As a Libertarian sort of guy, I have a strong attitude that people should be allowed to do what they like provided it doesn't hurt others. If they want to be fat pigs, fine--but don't blame others for your fat pigginess. So, one of the basic tenants of this film, that fast food is evil, is something I could care less about--eat it if you want and avoid it if you want. But, I was VERY happy when I saw that although the film clearly is anti-fast food, as least it did NOT go where I assumed--to try to legislate healthy eating--something that is anti-freedom all the way. On the other hand, the film did have some compelling arguments--even if it did not turn me into an anti-fast food sort of guy.Morgan Spurlock stars in the film--and that is THE reason I watched. I LOVED his documentary "The Greatest Movie Ever Sold". In "Super Size Me" he decides to eat nothing but McDonald's food for an entire month to see that might happen. The results were pretty impressive to watch--Spurlock put on almost 25 pounds and he felt miserable. Now few SANE people would eat fast food that much--but the health problems that were documented with multiple doctors and blood tests were very sobering. This sort of diet will kill you. Though, of course, no one will eat the same very limited range of foods Spurlock did (why didn't he eat McDonald's salads and who super-sizes every time?!).What I also really appreciated in the film was its discussion of school lunches. I am a retired teacher and hate some school districts which have taken every ounce of fun out of eating--banning cookies, cakes, etc.--even when brought in by students to celebrate birthdays and the like! Spurlock doesn't address this--but he does show the opposite--how some school lunches are essentially garbage--nothing but fatty crap with hardly any nutritional value. This was sobering and quite sad, as some kids receive almost all their nutrition from school meals.Overall, while I think Spurlock came down too hard on fast food and not nearly hard enough on personal responsibility, he was not a far-leftist who seemed unreasonable. He posed a lot of good questions and the film did make me think. I might have given it an 8, though scenes of his rectal exam and puking knocked off a point (YECCH!).</fullReview>

This documentary is a direct critique of fast-food restaurants in America (especially McDonalds) and also to the American way of life. In order to do this, Spurlock eats three times a day at McDonalds (breakfast, lunch and dinner) in one month. His aim: demonstrate how unhealthy that sort of food is by using his body as proof of the damage caused.To carry out his complaints Spurlock set several rules:He could only eat food served in that famous chain. He had to eat the whole meal. He had to have the super size menu when offered. He shouldn't walk more than 5,000 steps per day, which is the estimated amounts of steps that an American does.During the movie, the protagonist has three doctors that track the changes his body suffers. After that month, the doctors told him that his liver was severely damaged, his cholesterol was very high, and he significantly increased his weight among other consequences. In fact, he had eaten 90 meals which is more than the amount of junk food that a person should eat in eight years.The first time I watched this documentary was four years ago and I almost forgot the facts that Spurlock shows in it. I thought that after coming to the US it was a good moment to re-watch it. We have McDonalds, KFC, and many other fast food chains in Spain but it is not like here. Everything is one step further in America. For example, the medium size here is equivalent to the big size in Europe. Furthermore you are not able to re-fill your glass. You pay for a single drink and if you want more you have to purchase another one. I would love to have free refills in Spain but I think it is a very bad habit because it makes you over consume.I think that the US has a serious problem with food. People do not have time to go back home for lunch time and they end up going to some fast food restaurants because of the lack of options. In Spain, we go t fast food places as well but also it is possible to go to a normal restaurant where you can purchase a daily menu for nine dollars. It is a bit more expensive but healthier. Spurlock points out another issue: Americans do not walk at all. Those 5,000 steps are a very poor rate which provokes a lethal combination: unhealthy food and lack of exercise. When I first watched the movie I could not understand why people didn't walk but when I got here I saw what the cities are like. Mainly, they lack good public transportation system. Therefore, everyone has a car decreasing the exercise done daily. Finally, he was asked nine times if wanted a super size menu. Seven, out of those nine, were in Texas. Everything is bigger in Texas, even people. According Spurlock's research, Texas is the fattest state in the country. Thus, not walking and eating super size meals super sizes you.I think that the results of this documentary showed that Americans have to change some habits. However, it doesn't depend only on people's willing but also on the opportunities offered by those restaurants. Corporations have a lot of power and I think that it would be essential that the government establishes more controls over that market to protect people's health. Otherwise we will keep super sizing ourselves.</fullReview>

And it is a thoughtful way to end a heartfelt assessment about a major problem in the United States but also in Western Europe. One to muse on.One learns at the end of the documentary that the "super size" menu was definitely removed from the list of dishes available at McDonalds. 		<fullReview>Fascinating documentary of filmmaker Morgan Spurlock's self-imposed 'McDiet.' For one month, he limits himself to eating from the famous Golden Arches, tracking the gradual decline of his health and expansion of his waistline. The seemingly simple premise isn't for a moment dull, and his journey is guaranteed to make you think twice before picking up that greasy burger for supper. Spurlock also splices in an interesting look at the affect of fast food and commercialism on society as a whole, particularly on children. And unlike the work of Michael Moore -- to whom Spurlock has been compared -- none of it comes off as mean-spirited. Highly recommended.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Are you lovin' it?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As someone who works for what would be considered "fast food", I approached the documentary "Super Size Me" with some hesitation. I know that the movie's creator, Morgan Spurlock, chose McDonald's and not the fine establishment of In-N-Out, which I work for, to show how this food is terrible, but it still deals with the very food I work with day in and out (pun intended). I believe his intention to show how awful fast food has become is achieved, but I think there is a serious flaw in the way he does this. Yes fast food is terrible, but who eats it 3 times a day? NO ONE! Spurlock goes to the extreme for his cause, and decided he is going to go on a one month diet of only McDonald's: three meals a day, seven days a week. And to add to the fun, anytime he is asked if he wants to super size his meal, he MUST say yes. Spurlock begins by going to some "doctors" and getting checked out before he begins this suicide mission. His weight is a mere 186, and his cholesterol is a wonderful 165. In other words, he is in fine shape. Now, one thing that made me suspicious about this film was the doctors. You never know who is real or not. One might think Spurlock wouldn't use just actors, and the credits give the names of doctors playing themselves, but I still found it a little suspicious that his GP (general practitioner) was always wearing the same blue blazer with the black shirt underneath, and was always unshaven, with the same amount of shadow. After a month, you'd think he might have changed his clothes a couple of times and shaved, or not and have a beard. And as for the GI, we never see her actually doing anything. She is just always sitting behind the desk with her hands clasped, nodding her head. A little suspicious if you ask me. Anyway, back to the movie. Spurlock begins his day by going to Mickey D's for some breakfast. McMuffin sandwiches, which I must admit I've eaten before, but only out of desperation because I was hungry, and there was nothing else to eat. He continues his diet at lunch time, getting a double Quarter-Pounder with cheese meal and for dinner, non other than the Big Mac meal. Already, after just one day, he is starting to look a little ill, but he is really into it, cheering as he bites into the burgers. After 5 days, he goes back to the "doctors" to get another check up. He has gained 10 pounds already. His "GP" advises him to go to a nutritionist, who thinks Spurlock is insane for doing this diet, as does his girlfriend, and even Spurlock himself, but the cause is too important, and so Spurlock continues on! Along the way, we meet a colorful "cast" of character, from lunchroom workers, to middle school students, to diabetic patients receiving gastric bypass surgery. Perhaps the most interesting of them all is one Dan Gorske. Now, if you don't want to have the fun spoiled, and it is quite a shocker as to whom this man is, then I would skip down to the next paragraph now, otherwise, continue reading. Dan Gorske has set a record for eating Big Macs. He absolutely LOVES them. In his interview with Spurlock, he tells us that in one year he ate over 700 Big Macs. Now obviously there are only 365 days in a year, so this means he is eating 2-3 burgers a day! Spurlock is absolutely amazed, and probably a bit disgusted, as I was, but the most incredible thing was that this man was not obese. And it is later revealed that his cholesterol was normal. Pretty crazy if you ask me. After 12 days, Spurlock goes back in for another weigh in. His weight is 203 pounds. That means he has gained 17 pounds in 12 days! But it is not enough to stop Spurlock. He continues on! In the mean time, he gets sick, he has chest pains, and towards the end, his "doctors" tell him his liver is failing. He also experiences male "dysfunction" which his girlfriend complains about. On the whole, I would say it is well done. Some of the ways Spurlock filmed this were a tad annoying. The scenes jump so rapidly, that you don't have much time to ingest what is going on, (not that you would really want to anyway). The movie was also extremely washed out, which made it very difficult to focus on the details of the scenes. While I wouldn't necessarily say that I enjoyed this film, or liked it, I would recommend that everyone see it, at least once. It was very interesting, and if truthful, very scary as well. The funny thing is, we all know that McDonald's is junk, but we still go there. My manager at In-N-Out once said to me, "We expect them to serve us crap, but we still go." Why? One place I can recommend is In-N-Out. To quote their theme song:"That's what a hamburger's all about!!!"</fullReview>

		<username>Angus T. Cat</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Thought provoking film, but some questions left unanswered</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Supersize Me" is a thought provoking film that humorously but intelligently examines the fast food industry in the US, focusing on the leader in the field, McDonalds. It raises issues concerning how fast food is advertised and sold and the effects on health, but it leaves some questions unexamined.The film stresses that Spurlock gained over twenty pounds during his 30 day diet of McDonalds breakfast and lunch menus, his cholesterol levels skyrocketed, and his liver became so affected his doctor compared it to the liver of someone set to drink himself to death, like Nicholas Cage's character in Leaving Las Vegas. Yet the film contains an interview with Don Gorse, a man who is famous for eating at least one McDonalds Big Mac every day of his life since 1972. Gorse's image is seen in the sequence that caricatures McDonalds' "super heavy users"; people who eat McDonalds food three times or more per week. All the other super heavy customers are portrayed as exactly that, super heavy. Gorse is as thin as a rake. The end credits state his cholesterol level as 140, which seems low. Spurlock emphasizes that fast food dangerously increases the weight of many people who eat it frequently. Why then is Gorse skinny with no apparent cholesterol problems? Is it simply because he doesn't eat the fries? Also, the film includes information about the food industry in the US and how agents working for General Mills and other large food companies put pressure on Washington to advance their interests. Surely McDonalds, Burger King and the like are part of the problem, as the spokesman says... but so are General Mills, Kellogg's and other manufacturers. "Supersize Me" encourages the audience to skip the Golden Arches and eat at home, but what are they eating at home? Not all families have mothers like Spurlocks who cook from scratch every night, or people like Spurlock's wife who cook only organic vegan dishes. McDonalds is a bit of an easy target. I can see Spurlock's point that he chose an all McDonalds diet because of the lawsuit brought against it by the two teenage girls who blamed their obesity on Ronald McDonald. The film shows that it isn't just the fast food industry overwhelming TV with advertising... the McDonalds ads are joined by ads for candy, snacks, cereal and other sugar and fat laden products, many of them aimed specifically at young children.I would have liked to see more debate about the lack of education in schools regarding nutrition and the need for more physical education classes, more health classes, and classes in cooking (for both sexes). Most of all I would have liked to see more debate over the companion industry to the fast food and processed food industries, the diet industry. It would have been revealing to see how the fast food restaurants and food companies' sugary and high fat foods lead into the market for diet versions of the same foods.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Well made documentary on the perils of a fast-food life</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Morgan Spurlock directs, writes and stars in this, a frightening look at our dependence and addiction to fast foods. And here I am reviewing the DVD which offers great value in both the deleted scenes, extra scenes and a powerful interview with Eric Schlosser, author of "Fast Food Nation". He commences the movie by having a complete health evaluation by several professionals and is given a clean bill of health. His energy zooms from the screen. Added to this is his engaging commentator style, one gets to like Morgan Spurlock! He formulates a plan to eat three meals a day at McDonald's for one month. He also acquires a pedometer and resolves to do the same amount of exercise as an average American does. The plan is simple - he gets to eat every item on the menu at least once, and gets to SUPER SIZE if asked by a McDonald's employee. Within a week, the toll is being recorded on Morgan both physically, psychologically and addictively. He begins throwing the food up within a few days (a great warning) but soon he is depressed if he is not eating it. His body is slowing down, he is getting heart palpitations, he is seriously depressed and lethargic between feeds of his drug. He is affected sexually and his healthy girlfriend is becoming more than concerned about him and his changed personality. Added to this are his doctor's urging after only two weeks to stop the diet as he could die from the impact on his liver and have a cardiac arrest. But Morgan persists. I found his mix of humour and fairness appealing. For instance, he interviews a man who has lived on Big Macs for years and years with no adverse effects. However, the bottom line is that the real cost of McDonalds is never recorded. The low wages and no benefits, the garbage generated daily, the hooking of kids so early in life on a lifestyle that will shorten their lives not to mention affect the quality of their lives, the effect on the planet of the huge "mills" that it takes to generate all the pork and beef that McDonalds uses. One startling fact was that there could be bits of a 1000 cows in one burger. Now there's a thought. The Chicken Mcnuggets harbour bits we don't want to know about. And also the feed given to these poor captive animals is the leftovers from their slaughtered relatives. Happy Meals are anything but. And the shelf life of cooked fries from MacDonald's that are allowed to sit in a jar? No change after 10 weeks and still counting, they threw them out. Scary. 9 out of 10. It should be required watching for children. What I found particularly edifying were the solutions that Morgan offered, particularly the interviews with the high schools that had completely turned around their cafeterias and made gym a requirement for all students every day. Bravo, we need more such movies....</fullReview>

		<date>2005-06-21</date>
		<username>actionlad</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>You'll never McLook at McFast Food the McSame Again</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Super Size Me is an exploration of the obesity epidemic that has been sweeping the US over the last decade. The idea for this film seems to come from the many McLawsuits that have sprung up over the past couple years from people suing the fast food industry for making them fat... or so they claim. Did these people really become fat from eating McDonalds food? And if so, how much of that food would they have to consume to suffer the adverse effects of fast food? This documentary follows one man, Morgan Spurlock, on a mission to answer those questions and more. Spurlock (under the advice of not one, not two, but three physicians) decides to undergo a 30 day diet of eating nothing but food from the McDonalds menu. Spurlock puts a few restrictions on himself such as: if they ask him if he wants to "super-size" he has to say "yes." Also I think he had restrictions on how much physical activity he could do. There were a few other restrictions on how much he was able to do in regards to McDonalds food as well, but I can't remember them.What occurs over the next 30 days really is astounding in that Spurlock transforms his body into an unhealthy mass that is full of high cholesterol, high blood pressure and excess body fat. The changes are so dramatic that even the physicians and his nutritionist are amazed and all warn him to stop the diet before completing the last week. Spurlock pushes forth and ends up gaining almost 30 pounds in 30 days.I really enjoyed this film not only for its decent production values, but also because even though this is documenting reality, you really feel sympathy for Spurlock for what he is going through (depression, chest pains, relationship problems, etc). I feel that Spurlock hits home how harmful this food is for you if consumed on a regular or even semi-regular basis. He handles a lot of these difficulties with humor and a smile, but as time continues on, Spurlock's smile is less frequent and he's less excited about the idea of eating another McDonalds meal.The only real criticisms I have for this film are that Spurlock is a bit too sympathetic to the fatties that sued the fast food industry in painting them like unwilling victims who had no idea that a Big Mac and a large order of fries are going to put them in the grave faster. These are people who clearly don't want to accept any responsibility for their poor decisions and instead see a cash cow (pardon the pun) in the form of McDonalds.It makes sense why Spurlock would take aim at McDonalds as the sole purveyor of unhealthy food. I mean McDonalds is the leader in the fast food industry and has been since Ray Croc was president. I think this also ends up being the film's weakness since Burger King, Carl's Jr. and Taco Bell all have just as unhealthy offerings on their menus.Overall, a really well done film that seems to arouse in one's self utter disgust followed by a resolution to never touch fast food again.By the way, don't forget to check out the extras on the DVD - most notably, "The Smoking Fry."</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I have not eaten at McDonalds for about 3 years, now. This is not because of some weird diet thing that I have, or prejudices against the "Golden Arches" - I just haven't really felt the need."Super Size Me" is an entertaining documentary made by a brave man named Morgan Spurlock. His goal: to eat nothing but McDonalds three times a day, for thirty days straight, and to experiment on how our bodies would cope if we all did the same.Spurlock is either a genius or completely insane. Spurred on by the new rather worrying statistics about the obesity levels in the USA, he has taken the subject to heart. The end result is both interesting and clever. I do have one word of caution: DO NOT WATCH THIS MOVIE WHEN YOU ARE FEELING A LITTLE PECKISH!!Ahem.It works on both levels - you can be easily put off by the facts and figures, but then, when you see one of those burgers or fries or whatever he is eating at the time, you start doing really good impersonations of Homer J Simpson: "Mmmm. Burgers!"But Spurlock uses the opportunity to have a go at McDonalds to have an even bigger go at the whole of the fast food industry and even the school dinners that are fed to America's kids. Now, I'm from England, and just recently this chef named Jamie Oliver has been running around campaigning for healthier lunches. The results have been really positive, with very big promises made by the government because it affects everyone. I can't help wondering, after watching with horror the state of the lunches, whether someone should do the same in America. There was an example of a school that fed really healthy lunches to its pupils, and therefore has managed to fight back the misbehaviour of the students. Plus, states Spurlock, it costs the same as the current food programme set up!Spurlock is not as funny as Michael Moore, who you can instantly draw comparisons to. (Incidentally, I wonder how many times Moore goes to McDonalds a week?) However he does raise an issue and makes a film that everyone can take note of and watch. The trouble with some of Moore's work - say, for example, "Farenheit 9/11", which I wasn't too keen on if you read my review from that - is that he can be too anti-government. There is no problem with this in "Super Size Me".Overall the film is fun and a very good warning to us all. Now, if you'll excuse me, I'm going for a run. Just as soon as I've finished this quarter pounder with cheese...7/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Super Size Me (2004)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Morgan Spurlock has noticed, like many people, that Americans are getting bigger and bigger. After seeing how two young girls are planning on suing McDonalds for making them fat, Morgan decides to live out the dream of many an eight year old child and only eat at McDonalds for a whole month. The rules are as follows 1) He can only eat things supplied over the counter at McDonalds 2)He must have three square meals a day 3) He must have everything on the menu at least once over the next month and finally 4) If asked if he "Would like to super-size" his meal, he must reply "Yes". Many people seem to have a problem with this film because no one in their right mind would eat three meals a day at McDonalds, because everyone knows they are unhealthy. Maybe so but Morgan says this at the end of the film, this is an extreme situation. Let's face it it wouldn't be anywhere near as interesting if he ate four McDonalds meals a week over a long period of time. Morgan chooses McDonalds, not to be mean, but because it is the leader of the fast food world. Morgan supplies some not so shocking statistics, such as McDonalds serves more customers a day than the entire population of Spain. At the beginning Morgan is checked over by three doctors, all of whom seem confident about the experiment, and don't think anything too bad will happen. It is amazing to see how they change their minds, as Morgan piles on the pounds, and his cholesterol increases etc. During the film Morgan doesn't exercise, as he is attempting to simulate the average American. It is an interesting and entertaining documentary, Morgan comes across sometimes like Michael Moore as both are very humorous, but Morgan doesn't seem as biased. Morgan soon becomes addicted and is told he must stop and that his liver is turning into Pate. At no point does Morgan say we shouldn't eat McDonalds, but when he shows Americans who do not exercise, and go to McDonalds 4-5 times a week it is time to worry. Especially since McDonalds aims so much advertising at children, and the schools aren't exactly a healthy place for the people of tomorrow to start out. The film does go on a bit, after all we get the point after the first weigh in of how bad McDonalds food is, and it doesn't tell us anything new, apart from demonstrating the extreme dangers of fast food.</fullReview>

When I finally saw it, I was sickened. Not by the message, but the sight of the food and what it was doing to Morgan Spurlock. Due to economics, I didn't eat out much; but when I did, it was predominantly fast food. I don't want to go near the stuff anymore. I had started to reach that conclusion when I ate at a McDonalds on a recent trip, after dropping 30 pounds over the previous 6 months and eating more healthy foods. The food tasted awful, especially the fries. After watching this, I'll never go in a McDonalds again, nor am I likely to consume fast food.With good humor and relative objectivity, Spurlock shows the effect of a steady diet of fast food. Granted, he took it to the extreme by eating all meals there, which is not the norm, but there are a significant number of people who eat regularly at fast food restaurants. The damage they are doing to themselves is far less, but still detrimental. Spurlock makes it clear that people choose to consume this food, but that they are also bombarded with advertising from childhood onward to consume this product. He also documents the difficulty to obtain nutritional information in the restaurants and the amount of lobbying the industry does to protect itself from legislation that would require it to provide more honest information about its products. So how do consumers make informed choices if the necessary information isn't there for them?We watch as doctors and loved ones plead with Spurlock to quit, and the physiological problems that arise. He finds himself listless and depressed when not eating the food. He wakes up in the middle of the night with chest pains and shortness of breath. He feels odd sensations in his body. In the course of 30 days he gains approximately 25 pounds and loses muscle tone.The most damning aspects are the footage of school cafeterias, where students are fed a diet of french fries, chips, sugared drinks, fatty meat with heavy sugar sauces, and starchy vegetables. Nowhere do you see green vegetables and lean meat or vegetable protein. Not until you go to a school for troubled students, where a corporation or the FDA are not dictating the food. Instead, they have lean meats, plenty of fresh fruits and vegetables and the complete lack of soda. Only one state requires physical education classes, Illinois. Most students across the country are gaining little exercise, and consuming nothing but fat and simple carbohydrates.What's not seen in the film is the most sickening. In the DVD extras, is footage of an experiment Spurlock conducts. He places a Big Mac, Quarter Pounder, Fillet-O-Fish, Fries, a homemade restaurant burger, and freshly made french fries, in glass jars. Each is placed in a separate container and their breakdown is filmed over the course of several weeks. The homemade burger and fries turn moldy fairly quickly, while the McDonalds' food takes far longer. After EIGHT WEEKS, the McDonalds fries show no signs of mold! Ugh! Just think how long it takes your body to digest that, compared to potatoes that you cut and fry yourself!Now, there are many factors to consider in watching this. One, Spurock engages in no rigorous exercise. Two, he eats three meals per day from McDonalds. Three, although he tries everything on the menu, most of his meals are the worst foods. Four, he adds condiments to the foods items. Five, he supersizes the meal when asked. Taken in moderation, or with regular exercise, his weight gain would have probably been less significant, but would his cholesterol levels and heart rate been any lower?I don't think this film is necessarily a call for a vegan lifestyle, although his girlfriend makes that point. However, it is a wake-up call to America to put thought into what it eats and to engage in regular exercise. Sometimes, it takes the extreme to get people to act. See this film and act.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A Big Deal (Even a Super Sized Deal) For a Reason</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While those who say it's not well executed have a slight point, Morgan Spurlock does make a must-see film. The film doesn't do much to inspire other filmmakers within it's presentation, it is a perfect compliment and non-distraction to the film's real meal (pun intended) - it's genius content.Does a fast food franchise deserve to be sued for making customers obese? Is it solely to blame? Are people's beliefs about their food exaggerating, or horribly understated? These a re some of the questions Super Size Me asks. And, how to answer? Simple - make yourself the guinea pig and record every twist and turn along the way.Morgan begins an experiment which, to not give too much away, probably both doesn't meet but most of which exceeds your expectations. He'll eat Mickey D's 3 times a day, for 30 days, and tackle food and weight related issues, and the developing results, along the way. He can only Super Size it (note: no longer a possibility for us, as McDonald's removed the option for mega sized meals just more than a month after it's Sundance premiere - just a coincidence, of course . . . ) when asked at the counter, he must work through the entire menu, and can eat nothing not sold over the counter at McDonald's. On the way, he'll meat with three doctors, face the caring wrath of his vegan girlfriend, and meet some interesting people.Filled with intriguing ideas and some questions for the food industry that can hit as hard and leave people as stunned as Michael Moore on politics, Morgan goes from the state of health in schools and their cafeterias (where he helps the audience to draw up most of their own conclusions, especially from one interview he has with a teacher who expects way to much out of tens, and I say that as one myself), to the differences between the weight of his home city to another, and everything in between. What comes together is a movie that's well paced, and well if, again, not inspiringly and groundbreakingly executed, and filled attention grabbers in each scene. The DVD shows Spurlock has great directing instincts, and cut many interesting scenes which were simply too long or didn't add anything to either side of the argument. In the end, the suspense of the results pours on, and the movie becomes as addictive as it's subject matter (I was forced to watch the movie over two nights, and it killed me). By the end, Morgan has graced us with a little more than we wanted to know about the experiment, but enough to both terrify and amuse us on the way. It's a classic documentary, and for those few haters who will tear the movie apart for it's average composure, everyone has to admit that it's classic just for it's content, and the DVD will always be special to me for the Bonus Footage of the metamorphosis of some McSandwiches and Fries over the course of nearly 3 months. It's classic, and I don't know if this guy can top it - check it out, you won't be sorry.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I'd seen this film advertised at the marquee of my local theater for months and never saw it until it came out on video. On a whim, my girlfriend and I rented it last night. What an eye opener! I have long since sworn off fast food in favor of cooking real, unprocessed food, but mainly for the simple fact that I did not want to give my money to a corporation for food of mediocre quality. Morgan Spurlock's film reinforces the main reason to boycott fast food; it is unhealthy. Further, the might that fast food giants and other corporate interests wield on children is nothing short of shameful. During his introduction, he showed that there was an average of four McDonalds per square mile in Manhattan! More McDonalds than fire stations, police departments or public parks! Also, children are subjected to a staggering 10,000 advertisements a year! During an exercise with school children, most were unable to identify photos of George Washington, George Bush or a likeness of Jesus, but they sure knew the Wendy's girl and Ronald McDonald! The film follows Spurlock as he eats three meals a day at McDonalds, and is filled with factual interviews with health care professionals, dieticians, and a former Surgeon General of the United States. It is brilliant in its simplicity and at each new shocking revelation my girlfriend wondered aloud why Spurlock was not sued by McDonalds! To his defense, he did not slander or libel them in any way, but merely showed what happens to the human body and mind when subjected to an exclusive diet of McDonalds for thirty days. His accurate and comprehensive documentation of his exact state of health before during and after was commendable. I was shocked to learn the real and dangerous changes his body rapidly made. His liver deteriorated, his blood pressure rose and his cholesterol skyrocketed, not to mention the fact that he gained nearly 25 pounds in a short 30 days. Coincidentally, after this film was screened at the Sundance Film Festival, McDonald's stopped selling its 'Super Size' meals. This is a testament to the power of the independent filmmakers of the world. I applaud men like Morgan Spurlock and Michael Moore for using their artistic talents in addressing real issues in our society and not creating just another film in an endless stream of Hollywood tripe. Hopefully you will see this film and it will compel you to act.</fullReview>

"Supersize me" has often been compared with Michael Moore's work, and some people feel it's better than Farenheit9/11. I don't. No good documentary is unbiased, anybody who tells you that is lying. Be it moderate or extreme, the film should reflect the makers opinion in the best possible way. This one is no exception, just ask a McDonalds spokesperson. Morgan tells his story with wit and honesty, but he is definitively biased, and he lacks Moore's ability to find the surreal and the absurd. 		<fullReview>This documentary film targets specifically MacDonalds, but also attacks the wider problem of obesity in the United States and the extent to which fast food id responsible.After hearing about two teenagers trying to sue MacDonalds for causing their obesity, New York film-maker Morgan Spurlock decides to eat nothing but MacDonalds food for thirty days, to see how justified the claims are. The rules of his experiment are: He can only eat food on the MacDonalds menu, he must try everything on the menu at least once during the experiment and finally he must "super-size" his order if asked.Before the experiment Spurlock (whose girlfriend is a vegan chef) is a picture of health, and the film documents the toll the experiment takes on his body.The news that MacDonalds food isn't exactly healthy, can surely come as no surprise to anyone. People do have a choice whether or not to eat healthily, and, of course, most people don't eat every single meal in a fast-food restaurant. But the most effective scenes in the film show the havoc that the whole fast-food culture wrecks on people's health. not just MacDonalds but the food that is served in schools as well. The film is very entertaining, with Spurlock coming across as a more likable version of Michael Moore. I am one of the many people who enjoys eating fast-food and, although this film will certainly make me think a bit before going into my local fast-food outlet, I won't be going on a diet just yet.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A one line diatribe that is entertaining and possibly even effective</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In a similar style to Michael Moore's diatribes (against firearms of the U.S. government), Super Size Me takes a heavyweight pot-shot at the fast food industry ? notably McDonalds. Morgan Spurlock looks at the general wailing against fast-foods and their disastrous effect on health and obesity. Under the watchful eye of several doctors and his vegan girlfriend, he embarks on a month-long regime where he will eat and drink McDonalds and nothing else. As expected, his health goes from very good to pretty awful. Super Size Me is a bit of a one-line rant, but entertainingly and effectively shot. If it doesn't put you off fast food maybe nothing will. In the closing credits, the film points out that McDonalds altered their marketing a few weeks after the film opened at the Sundance Festival, pushing apparently 'healthy' foods and revoking the 'super-sizing' offer (which allowed an extra big upgrade for a nominal amount). So it may have had some social impact already (which McDonalds, of course, deny). The film is funny and disquieting, though hardly rigorous. Perhaps the most unsettling fact it makes plain is the masses of corporate control over unhealthy meals in the school, and the powerlessness of parents.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I went to see this film shortly before parting to Texas for some work, and I came out knowing rather more about local eating habits than I expected. I must admit to now reserving a hotel room with a kitchen so I can do my own cooking and avoid the LardFest that seemingly awaits me.The documentary itself has a lot of things going for it - the form of the piece is very straightforward, and our host and his girlfriend are likeable and easy going. I particularly enjoyed the interaction with the team of doctors and experts who over the course of the film were seem gawping in horror, warning sternly to stop, and finally shaking their heads in disbelief as the rollercoaster ride of saturated fat consumption continued.Also effective are the sequences of statistics (all performed with cute graphics and suitably twisted soundtrack) and the sections where our man visits the schools and colleges that churn out the next generation of belt-busting heart attack victims. Looking at the school restaurant manager who convinced herself that the children who just bought fries were really sharing them and had brought their own brown bag lunch, or the face of the "cook" who no longer had to do much except open boxes and reheat gunk, you couldn't help but feel the decline of the United States was already well under way.Tellingly, in a sequence with the lobby group for the grocery trade, a spokesman let slip 'that we are part of the problem'. The look on his face was worth a thousand pictures, as he realized what he had said was now captured on film. Unsurprisingly he is no longer employed there.Finally, the whole thing slides along at a pleasant pace, there is no sense of 'I have a message and I am going to pound you with it for 2 hours' that I sometimes feel with a certain Mr Moore. All round a great piece and worthwhile watching just for the smug (in my case) feeling that a) I am from Europe b) I don't eat fast food c) I take exercise. Enjoy the thought as you shove another chocolate in your mouth at the cinema.9 out of 10.</fullReview>

		<username>(molasses84@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Indoctrinating children</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>My mother rarely cooked. My family had a rotating junk food roster: Dominos, Red Rooster, the sort of Chinese takeaway that includes prawn crackers with your order. I was intimate with the junk food circuit ? the plastic trays, the fluorescent lights and greasy bags. As an account of that intimacy, Super Size Me begins perfectly. A chorus of smiling children chant the names of fast food joints - "McDonald's, McDonald's, Kentucky Fried Chicken and a Pizza Hut." (I remember going through the motions at school ? hands tucked under my arms for KFC, the Pizza Hut roof made over my head.) Junk food's penetration of the youth market is a given, as is the particular joy a visit to McDonald's holds for a child. The film joins the dots on the way McDonald's actively recruits children: the playgrounds, the birthday parties, the warm early memories of eating with one's family. In one scene, director Morgan Spurlock holds out images for children to identify: they recognise Ronald McDonald more readily than Jesus or George Washington. He draws on our concern for children. He visits school cafeterias ? in the States, largely given over to junk food ? and physical education programs, draws our attention to the effect of obesity on self-image. The usual rhetoric about consumer choice ? no one forces you to eat junk food ? seems dishonest when you are indoctrinating children. (Give us your children and they are ours for life.)Michael Moore's strength as a filmmaker is his tendency to dramatise his material. In Bowling for Columbine, when Moore gets a gun with a new bank account, it is a perfect demonstration of the mad availability of firearms. Spurlock was evidently watching. He hit on a great conceit: for a whole month he eats nothing but McDonald's. With the assistance of a battery of doctors, he measures the effect on his health. The figures speak for themselves. In a fortnight, he gains ten percent of his body weight. (We watch as he steadily develops a paunch.) He does serious damage to his liver: the doctors liken his diet to an alcoholic binge. With disarming frankness, his girlfriend confides that their sex life has also suffered. Meanwhile, we meet an assortment of lawyers, school headmasters, teenagers ? and a man famous for his prolific intake of Big Macs. (He has eaten almost 18,000.) His local McDonalds keeps a running total on their sign outside. This is a fun and ultimately scary essay on American excess. We recoil in pleasurable disgust at every burger and half-gallon of soda.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Fantastic, educational look at a troubling American epidemic...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>***POSSIBLE SPOILERS*** I have rarely taken the opportunity to view documentary films, films which detail particular problems, subjects, and daily life in the eye of the camera. Call me crazy for those big budget, major concept films, who discriminates the like of these independent insights, but I usually would rather engage in the discovery channel for this kind of matter. However, I was instantly interested and intrigued by the subject of this documentary, focusing on the hazardous, unfortunate, augmenting epidemic which is obesity in America and the drastic, perhaps horrifying effects an unhealthy diet produces upon even the most healthy individual. Spurlock, a masterful, innovative director in producing insightful documentary, questions the why and the how about America's disturbing "fat formation", and even embarks upon a personal experimentation wherein he will indulge nothing but high-calorie, unhealthy McDonald's food for the duration of 30 days. Preceding his experiment, Spurlock ventures into the world of the health department to assess his health- blood pressure, heart rate, weight; the essentials in maintaining proper health. He is informed that his health is perfectly fine and that his future "diet" could alter some components of his health, yet nothing of grave significance. Throughout the film, Spurlock indulges incessantly in McDonald's food, to the extent of obsession and health defection. He discovers that various aspects of his once healthy cycle have been drastically, regrettably altered, namely his liver, which has been abused as severely as that of an alcoholic (One doctor even links his liver to that of Nicholas Cage in Leaving Las Vegas, which eventually results in tragic cirrhosis death). Simultaneously on his journey of experimentation, Spurlock delves into America's parallel problem of constantly feasting in the most insalubrious of foods, as the epidemic of obesity has now become, to some degree, a normalcy among many communities (i.e. Houston), and has become just shy of the leading cause of preventable death in the U.S., behind only tobacco. Spurlock examines this problem so productively and effectively, with humor and serious attention, through statistics, pictures, graphs, interviews, etc., and discovers for himself and for the audience, that fast food is literally sickening, especially excessively, and in conclusion, poses the provocative and educated question, "Do you want to live in misery, in sickliness, in eating fast food and literally destroying yourself(statistically said to be the leading cause of the current obesity epidemic), or would you rather wake up, face the facts, and avoid any such health defects as were evidenced by Spurlock's shocking experimentation? McDonald's, a corporation supposedly interested in only its customers and their happiness, rejected to even interview Spurlock for a discussion of the repercussions of "McMeals". Look out for yourself, and regard Spurlock for a critical, scary example of the effects of dangerous, malicious "fat" food. **** out of ****</fullReview>

		<date>2005-11-23</date>
		<username>(roland@atkinsononfilm.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>McBinge, McFix, McPoison, McFat, McSick, McDie (?)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First time writer-director Morgan Spurlock seized the right moment to ride the wave of public interest in our national epidemic of obesity and overweight, and the important role of popular reliance on fast foods in contributing to this problem.As everybody knows by now, Mr. Spurlock, a fit fellow in his early 30s, dedicated himself to a month of eating exclusively at McDonalds. His self imposed rules: 3 meals every day, trying every item on the menu at least once; saying yes whenever a clerk asked if he wanted to alter ("upgrade") his order to the "Super Size"; and eating everything he ordered.He was initially evaluated by his primary care physician, a gastroenterologist , a cardiologist, a registered dietician and an exercise physiologist. They also checked him at regular intervals during and for months following his binge. Up till this binge, he ate sensibly (his partner is a vegan), had only 11% body fat and ran a cholesterol of 165. At the end of a month, having eaten at McDonald outlets in Manhattan (where there are 83 vendors, we learn), California and Texas, he had gained over 25 pounds, his body fat was up to 18%, and his cholesterol was now 225! Moreover, he showed as much as 10-fold increases in hepatic enzymes, indicating acute inflammation of liver cells, most likely due to acute fatty liver. His blood pressure had risen significantly. He was having regular headaches, fatigue and depressed mood, all of which could only be reversed by the next McMeal. His libido had faded to nothing.Spurlock is the first to admit that his binge was extreme. Even the most devoted consumers at the Golden Arches probably don't eat three full meals a day there, day in, day out (though fast food has become the main food source for an increasing number of citizens). Still, the symptoms he rapidly acquired and the biochemical changes that occurred serve as impressive evidence of the toxicity of the McDonald diet.Arguably the standard fare there also has an addictive effect, since the relief of discomforting symptoms (headache, dysphoria, depression) is brought about by the next McFix. I'm not enthused by our culture of victimization with its attendant lawsuits against big tobacco and other corporate giants for destructive personal habits that can by curbed by addicted individuals. But I think that the facts of Spurlock's experience provide as much justification for holding the fast food industry culpable and responsible for poisoning America as is the case for cigarette manufacturers.Spurlock nicely meshes segments on his own McBinge odyssey with useful information on weight, diet and health from talking heads like the former US Surgeon General, David Satcher. For anyone still in the dark about it, there is plenty here on the multiple health hazards of overeating and overweight. Spurlock has done a fine piece of public service here, packaging critically important health messages within an engrossing and often humorous personal story.By the way, it took 8 weeks for his liver and cholesterol test results to return to normal, and 14 months to take off all the weight he had put on. Whew! My rating: 8/10 (B+). (Seen on 04/01/05). If you'd like to read more of my reviews, send me a message for directions to my websites.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Convinced Me</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I wasn't someone who really needed to be convinced that eating fast food was bad for you, and yet Morgan Spurlock's film managed to convince me all over again. The shocking thing about this documentary is not that a diet of McDonald's turns out to be bad for you (wow, what a surprise!) or fattening (get out of town!) but that it actually acts like a poison to your system. Even the doctors in the film are amazed at the detrimental effects a fast food diet had on Spurlock over the course of a month. He had as much fast food in 30 days that nutritionists recommend having in 8 years. That's a cold wake up call to those who defend themselves by saying things like, "I only have it once a week or so, so I'm o.k." Do the math--once a week equals 52 times in a year; at that pace, you'll eat 8 years' worth of fast food in under two.People also claim that this film has too firm an agenda, Morgan Spurlock is too preachy, etc., etc. I honestly don't understand these comments. Since when are documentaries allowed to be only objective in tone? Don't these people know that some of the best documentaries in film history have had clear agendas and clear points of view. This criticism is leveled at Michael Moore too. But if the film maker isn't allowed to have a point of view, why would he be making the film in the first place? "Super Size Me" is almost a horror movie in the effect it has on your psyche and your stomach. How anyone can watch this and continue passively to fill their bodies day after day with garbage is beyond me.Grade: A-</fullReview>

		<username>moogyboy</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>the McPukes, and more...not all of it good</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Morgan Spurlock has done something akin to what Quentin Tarantino did in Pulp Fiction: he made the act of biting into a hamburger look and sound absolutely disgusting. Literally, puke-inducing.We all know the story by now--Spurlock, tall, skinny, in exemplary health, decides to see what happens when he emulates in extremis the dietary habits of a lot of Americans by eating nothing but McDonalds products for a whole month. And of course, whenever possible, he SUPER SIZED his meals. Well, DUH. He gains about 20 lbs, his cholesterol and body chemistry go wacky, he gets moody, exhausted, and--horrors!--he can't even get it up for his annoying, supercilious vegan girlfriend.Actually, by the end of the movie, he didn't look all that fat to me; if Morgan Spurlock at day 30 of his McDiet is what's considered obesity by today's standards, we're in a lot of trouble.Super Size Me has some problems, but it's an immensely entertaining movie, if you can get around the washed-out camcorder-quality picture and sound, which is why I give it an 8. Spurlock comes up with a lot of eye-opening information and probably does it better than the more mean-spirited, arguably fact-challenged (but equally entertaining and probably justified) character bashing of Michael Moore. You do come away from the movie realizing just how insidious the McDonalds empire, and the manufactured food industry as a whole, really is. If you find yourself wanting to punch Ronald McDonald in the mouth after seeing this, you're probably not alone. He deserves it.In a way, I wish Spurlock had been more surgically focused on that aspect a'la Moore. Unfortunately a lot of the movie revolves around his mugging for the camera while stuffing his face--but I can't blame him for liking to turn the camera on himself, and watching his transformation is pretty startling and at times highly disturbing. I just can't get around the attention-seeking media stunt aspect of Spurlock's experiment.What bothers me a lot more is the movie's attitude toward overweight people in general. Spurlock, as I said a tall slender white guy, unrelentingly portrays fat people as something akin to carnival freaks; he seems to show not one bit of sympathy for them as people, or even to consider them as quite human...fat people are portrayed as faintly amusing objects of contempt. He almost negates his central thesis--that McDonalds makes billions in profits by getting people addicted to unhealthy food and the unhealthy lifestyles centered around its products--when he all but blames their customers for being so stupid to fall for it. And like so much of the media, Spurlock focuses tightly on obese people's physical appearances--fat bodies (especially women) as the epitome of grotesque, practically joshing us to shout "EEEWWWWWW!!!" in unison. Personally, his very skinny blonde girlfriend, she of the kelp quiche and organic salads, is more disgusting than any of the fat people in the movie. To his credit, he BRIEFLY disses the diet and fashion industries and espouses exercise and healthy eating instead. But one gets the sense that he's confused obesity with the people it afflicts, and that he'd like to whack them both from the face of the earth. It seems that underlying his ostensibly good intentions is some serious, perhaps unconscious, prejudice. I don't appreciate that.The blow-dried flack for the food industry's lobby at one point admits (to Spurlock's delight) that the industry is part of the problem. Unfortunaly, although he's produced a very entertaining and provocative documentary about a nefarious industry and the poor health they promote, so in part is Morgan Spurlock. But this isn't a political forum. It's about movies. And Super Size Me is, despite its few but serious flaws, a well-made one.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>First, let me say that I like Spurlock's style more than Michael Moore's because it is less accusatory, but I have enjoyed Moore's works as well.The issue I take with this experiment, is that even for an extreme, it's extreme. For breakfast, he would normally have a couple of sandwiches and a hash brown, and for the other meals he would order enough food to literally make himself sick on at least one occasion. 		<shortDescription>An astounding insight into the real dangers of fast food - No more!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The problem with writing about movies all the time is that you find out so much about it before it's even finished filming, so that when you go to see it there's often an anti climax or, if there is a surprise, no surprise at all. It can be quite frustrating not to see a movie afresh and your expectations be lifted or even shattered.The interesting thing about this movie is that it did the exact opposite, and it brought back this feeling. Within the first twenty minutes I was stunned at what was happening to this man and the facts and figures that were revealed.The opening of the movie bombards you with some astounding figures about the state of health of us all, and where the highest statistics of obesity are and how they've grown over the years. Well I say us all, but it is an American documentary.After that the film maker reveals his premise. He's going to eat McDonalds for a month and see what harm it will do him. Extreme, he agrees, and he totally acknowledges the rights of the individual to choice - they can easily walk past the fast food outlet - but then he has just as much ammunition for why you don't.The effects on the body and personality are amazing to watch, and quite frankly astounding. What really grabs you and wakes you up to the dangerous qualities of this diet are the Doctors involved in monitoring his health on a regular basis. They all begin on a quite relaxed and optimistic note, some body levels of fat and carbohydrate may rise but there shouldn't be a problem.Within weeks they've totally changed their opinion and the warning signs are beyond the dangerous levels. It actually gets to the point that the Doctor compares him to an alcoholic and warns him to stop before he does himself permanent damage, or even kills himself. Even they are astounded at the effects on his body.Morgan Spurlock is indeed a brave man, and his desire to show the perils of fast food, the mixed and blatantly wrong messages from both the Government and the industry itself and the general state of health of his country drive him to continue with the experiment.This is compelling watching and full of logically, well presented facts which cannot fail to persuade you what and who are at fault. The simple courtroom admissions of the fast food companies are shocking, and leave you wondering if the convenience is at all worth it.Without a doubt you have to watch this movie and understand what this food is doing to our bodies. Not only that, but to see what the new so called healthy options are, and are not, delivering to you.The only negative things I'd say about this movie are that it's quite clear that Spurlock is an extremely healthy man before going into this documentary, and this presents a far too good starting example of his fellow American. However, pretty soon, as he gets used to the amount of food, you realise it's actually a really good starting point, a baseline if you like.There's also a distinct lack of what the other options should be. A brief mention of a man who has been through the Subway diet doesn't make any comparisons or conclusions about that type of food. I think with so many people seeing this movie who can't decide for themselves not to go into a fast food establishment, it would have been good to hand feed them what is a better option, and why.</fullReview>

		<username>dee.reid</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>"Super Size Me" - Something very strange is going on here...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have a confession to make: I love (loathe) McDonald's food. I especially love the long lines, the noisy kids, and general rush of the fast-food environment.Food-wise at McDonald's, I love myself a sausage, egg &amp; cheese McMuffin, with two hash browns, and an orange juice. In fact, I loved it so much, that I can recall back during the summer of 1995, when I was 10 years-old, that every Monday and Wednesday I'd order this exact same meal for breakfast.Well, my shameless self-indulgence eventually caught up with me and when I was in the sixth grade three years later, I often faced the ridicule of a few select classmates, who called me fat names and such, despite the fact that I hadn't put on any real weight (in truth, at this time, I only weighed about 130 pounds, and that was normal for me). And I owe this normal weight to a quick metabolism (thank you, Dad).By the time I was 14, my doctor had diagnosed me with high blood pressure, most likely brought on from lack of exercise and eating too much fast-food (again, at this time I'm not at all overweight nor am I obese; I am still average size). So I start lifting weights and doing other fitness activities in an attempt to stay in shape, and I've been doing that ever since. I still eat fast-food about once or twice a week, but due to the fact that I never seem to have enough time to exercise anymore, I've had to cut a meal out of my diet. (Good-bye, lunch.)Evidently, America is not following the same pattern (or anything close to it), and filmmaker Morgan Spurlock made "Super Size Me" in 2004, in which he eats nothing but McDonald's food for 30 days straight in an attempt to get to the bottom of America's obesity epidemic. Even more frighteningly, obesity now sits close to the top of medicine's list of most preventable causes of death in America, almost ready to knock down smoking to the #2 spot.McDonald's track record of poor-quality foods has been well documented, and a lot of what we see in "Super Size Me" may not be new to most of us, as we are continually bombarded in the media on a daily basis by the number of heart attacks, diabetes, and other illnesses related to the mass consumption of their food (or any fast-food for matter).It's funny and saddening to see Spurlock get sick as a dog while consuming McDonald's food for the duration of this experiment, as he also makes a number of interesting points about the role the corporations play in America's food consumption. He's quick to note that most people are smart enough to know how to eat right, but also acknowledges that the restaurants themselves have an obligation to point out the nutritional value of their products. Why do you think so many fast-food restaurants now have nutrition charts (but does anybody actually bother to read them)? And McDonald's healthy choice menus are also exposed as being something of a sham, since according to Spurlock, their salads contain just as much fat and calories as a Big Mac. (This film also reaffirms my beliefs of just how unsafe their food truly is.)While "Super Size Me" may not be as bombastic or as confrontational as anything that Michael Moore has done in recent years, Spurlock's film is not really an indictment of McDonald's or any fast-food network, but is really more of a blazing and dense critique of American tastes (no pun intended) in food.Spurlock also explores the effect of obesity in children, as he points out how the companies often threaten the schools with withdrawal of funding if their products are not sold in the cafeterias. Ironically, the kids in the alternative education schools get the better end of the stick, as they many times get the real healthy foods such as fresh fruits, vegetables, and freshly cooked meals, not frozen stuff that comes in a box and can be opened with a box cutter. And he also discusses how Illinois has required physical fitness classes for grades K-12.As Spurlock hits the road across the country, he also shows us just how BIG America is becoming, and that the megalomaniacs at McDonald's are everywhere, with 83 locations on the island of Manhattan alone. Whoa. He also takes time out to discuss the case of the two girls who tried unsuccessfully to bring up a lawsuit against McDonald's for their weight problems, and parallels this story against his experiment.Morgan Spurlock has made a damning movie about American tastes, but you know how stubborn we Americans are, we don't like being told that there's something wrong with our country or with the people living in it. He made a movie that will hopefully open our eyes to the problem of our health in this country.8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>SPOILERS In the world today, weight as an issue is a major talking point. Like smoking, drinking and countless other problems, Obesity is everywhere. It's difficult to think of any information he could really show to demonstrate the other side of the argument, but there must be one."Super Size Me" is not a particularly good documentary. Whether his medium is flawed or not, Spurlock is trying to remind us of the problems the world has with obesity and how we must try to save ourselves. 		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A worthy sacrifice</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've always felt that documentaries are best done when they think small; Hoop Dreams follows two individuals, Fog of War is one person's life, and Supersize Me is one man's search for truth. The problem with, by contrast, Michael Moore is that he tries to encompass entire geopolitical issues into a feature length film, and by necessity must abridge and simplify. By focusing instead on one person's experience with one restaurant for one month, Morgan Spurlock allows his film room to grow in unexpected directions. Take as an example the man he encounters who has eaten at least two Big Macs a day since his teens and boasts a cholesterol level in the low 100's (hint: avoid the fries). If Moore had presented a normal American who had benefited from Bush's policies, and there are many, perhaps his film would have benefited. Some have accused Spurlock of being shamelessly self-promoting exhibitionist like Moore, and to an extent this is true, but the difference lies in the approach; Supersize Me takes as removed a position to the fast food industry as could be expected, while Fahrenheit 911 makes no secret of its predisposed enmity to its subject.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The most important point to remember is that I used to occasionally go into McDo's, BK, KFC, whatever, and then I saw this film. I don't want to eat something that showed no sign of decay after ten weeks, and which, if they hadn't accidentally been thrown out by an intern, probably would still be there, looking just the same...Another thing I found deeply disturbing was the segment on American school lunches. Morgan Spurlock visited a number of schools, and had a conversation with the Food Services Director at a school cafeteria in Naperville Illinois, and she was obviously *totally* oblivious to what the kids were doing, and totally unconcerned when Morgan Spurlock pointed out that the kids were buying junk food (crisps, fries, chocolates, sugared lemonade drinks). She even said that most of them bring a bag lunch, but it was clear as the camera wandered around the room that what these kids were buying was what they were eating - no bag lunches for these kids... He then visited a school for disturbed teenagers in Wisconsin, where the food was all prepared fresh in the school. Good old-fashioned food, and it looked infinitely more appealing than the junk in the Naperville school.I was riveted to the screen from the word go. Morgan Spurlock is a very good presenter, and the mix of footage was excellently chosen. The repeated theme of Morgan ordering yet another McMeal was broken up excellently by interviews by all sorts - his doctors, the former Surgeon General, the most honest lawyer you'll ever see ("you mean besides the money?"), a couple who had collected *every* bit of McDo's merchandise ever produced (UK DVD extra), and so on.This film was a real eye-opener, and I'll not forget it...</fullReview>

		<username>benri4761</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Not what people assume it to be</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>To most individuals who have not taken the time to actually sit down and watch the film "Super Size Me," Morgan Spurlock's chronicle of his own self-destructive diet is simply the film about how McDonald's is responsible for American obesity. This is anything but true. Rather than blame McDonald's, Spurlock simply uses McDonald's, as the largest fast food chain the world, as a very visible example to make a larger (no pun intended) point about the American diet in general.Though this film hardly portrays McDonald's in a positive light, it also criticizes those who exercise no personal responsibility in the management of their own diets, as well as the overall attitude towards exercise and healthy diet within the United States. He depicts individuals exercising no discretion in their food intake, as well as the disregard shown to school lunch and physical education programs throughout the country. Meanwhile, Spurlock paints a picture of the role that corporate food producers play in all sectors of the food market in America. Most notably, Spurlock examines the lobbying power that junk food companies exercise in Washington as opposed to the power of their healthier counterparts, and portrays the iconic status that fast food restaurants have reached in the minds of American consumers.In the end, Spurlock raises many crucial questions about the connection between the general lack of concern about health that runs rampant in American culture. And, as a unmistakable corporate icon, the Golden Arches simply serve as a perfect vehicle to illustrate this point.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Spurlock's stark warning that, yes, food can be as dangerous as tobacco.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film, certainly is based on the common-sense assumption that fast food is bad for you, and yet is deeply polarizing. For this reason, but not only this reason, I rated Morgan Spurlock's "Super Size Me" a 10. Spurlock's style, as captured throughout, is not unlike that of alleged Guerilla Journalist Michael Moore, but with a kind of youthful penchant for the extreme in his approach to subject matter. Spurlock's undertaking of a "month long McDonald's binge" (as so characterized by his cardiologist)proves as a dangerous physical experiment, and thus proves strongly and thoroughly his point with visual and statistical evidence. (Something that Moore often does with less care) Most Interesting was Spurlock's apparent addiction to and dependency on the fat and calorie rich-food. This demonstrated clear similarities between unhealthy food and tobacco. (Thus adding weight, no pun intended, to the arguments of the obese girls whose parents sued McDonald's.) Moreover, his asides are equally as colorful as the "Farenheit 9/11" director.Notably, Spurlock attacked the Corporate "Big Food" lobby of which Democratic Presidential candidate John Kerry is directly involved through his marriage to Teresa Heinz. 		<username>moonspinner55</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Morgan Spurlock charts a fast-food course for his own demise...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Healthy documentarian in New York City, spurred on by a lawsuit against the fast-food chain McDonald's by two overweight teen girls, decides to go on a junk food eating binge--having breakfast, lunch and dinner at Mickey D's for 30 straight days--to prove not only how unhealthy the food is, but unhealthy our collective eating habits are. Morgan Spurlock targets not only McDonald's in his self-assigned task, but also the food suppliers for America's elementary and high schools, most of whom are drowning today's youth in sugar and high cholesterol. Two-thirds of the way through his experiment, Spurlock's own cholesterol has skyrocketed, his weight is up substantially, and his liver is on the brink of malfunctioning. "Super Size Me" is certainly an informative and thought-provoking documentary, paced in a breezy, lightly amusing style, yet one begins to wonder about Spurlock's main impetus and agenda behind this potentially life-damaging high-fat diet; frequently using TV commercials (without their soundtracks) and animation to underline his points, he intentionally (and crassly) becomes a purveyor of shock value (which is where the footage from a gastric bypass comes in). Spurlock himself is an easy-going presence, and he's the perfect Everyman to host this film, but what are his motives? Is really he really concerned about the health habits of our population? The many faceless shots of men and women wandering around town with big bottoms and flabby tummies are not shown to be benefiting from any of Spurlock's overall denunciations (they're just visual targets) and the segues to the medical personnel don't do much to spike the proceedings (the doctors seem as worried about Spurlock as Spurlock is about the rest of us). In the end, the film is a skin-deep examination of a worrisome issue. McDonald's Restaurants come off not as cause or effect, but as a middlemen: a high-fat supplier we welcome into our lives. Personally, I prefer Del Taco. **1/2 from ****</fullReview>
	</review>

That's right; and entire MONTH of nothing but McDonald's. Three square meals a day, all bought from a McDonald's fast food restaurant establishment. As Morgan indulges in what he describes as "every eight-year-old's dream", he researches the causes of obesity all over the country. 		<username>ironhorse_iv</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>It's time for a food fight and I'll be loving it! Super-Size Me was a pretty good documentary. Still, it's without controversy.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Independent filmmaker, Morgan Spurlock is no Upton Sinclair. Directed and starring, himself. The film follows a 30-day period in which Morgan spent his time, eating only McDonald's fast food meals. The film documents this lifestyle's drastic effect on Spurlock's physical and psychological well-being, and explores the fast food industry's corporate influence, including how it encourages poor nutrition for its own profit. For a long time, for example, McDonalds claimed its fries were vegetarian, even as it continued to flavor them with beef tallow or how they defaulted on a promise to cut the trans-fats from its food. The company apologized, and paid a settlement. I'm not for or against McDonalds, nor a paid spokesmen for them, but in my opinion, this documentary gives nothing new. He's basically telling the audience something everyone already should know already: Fast food is bad for you if you eat it all the time. It felt very regurgitation of common knowledge. As an informative documentary, it fails to be, that good, due to a lot of misinformation and some outright lies in his studies. While, the movie makes it very clear that the rules of Morgan's experiment are: for 30 days, he can't eat or drink anything that isn't on a McDonald's menu. 2. He must eat three meals a day ? breakfast, lunch, and dinner. 3. He must consume everything on the menu at least once. 4. If a cashier asks Morgan if he wants to "super-size" his meal, he must agree. 5. He will attempt to walk about as much as (or rather, not substantially more than) a typical U.S citizen, based on a suggested figure of 5,000 steps per day. 6. He must consume everything on his plate. Still, some people has accused him of outright fraud with his McDonald results, claiming that Spurlock over did it, resulting in some shoddy diet research. One good example is the scene of Spurlock vomiting in the parking lot after his first Super-Sized meal. It probably has less to do with McDonalds being unhealthy, and more with Spurlock forcing himself to eat every single last bite of the meal long past when he was beginning to feel unwell. I really doubt, the average American is forcing themselves to over eat like that. Another is Morgan's normal diet. He admits that he practically never ate out before making the documentary, and that he normally barely even eats meat in his evening meals because his girlfriend is a vegan chef. It's inevitable that somebody who goes from this kind of diet to eating nothing, but McDonalds will have a much more extreme reaction than an average person who eats fast food in moderation. It sucks, that Morgan has refused to make his food logs public, possibly because even basic math indicates that he could not have followed his own rules and consumed 5,000 calories per day as he claimed. More scientific and less informal studies have found it impossible to replicate his results. It doesn't help that eating 5,000 calories per day of absolutely any sort of food, with limited exercise, is bad for you because eating excessive amounts of food while avoiding exercise is what causes all weight gain. All his rules for the experiment doesn't match with the results of normal fast-food eater, but an extreme case of overdoing it. A better research result came from the 2009's documentary film, Fat Head. I give the movie, some credit as Spurlock visits renowned Big Mac fanboy Don Gorske, who achieved notoriety for eating at least one Big Mac a day for well over a decade. In contrast to the film's message, it shows that the Gorske is actually in perfect health. It shows that the McDonald diet, can have different results on different people. Still, Morgan doesn't show too much, as he kinda self-censor an interviewer by having him cover his McDonald's salad from the camera. Despite a lot of poor sourcing informative, this was a funny and entertaining documentary. It remind me so much of a Michael Moore's style of documentary filmmaking, full of animation, pop culture references and great use of classy music. The different between them, is that Spurlock replaces Moore's smugness agenda with a self-deprecating approach. Super-Size Me is indeed more personal than Moore's political type movies. In fact, Super Size Me comes across as apolitical. Blaming both corporation, and those who pursue frivolous lawsuits against McDonald's, outing their lawyers' motives at being purely financial. Today, Spurlock might be more political as he wants tight government controls over how food companies market their products. But at the time, Spurlock's oeuvre far suggests that this was made as a publicity stunt than a political statement. It wasn't long after that that his TV show built on a similar premise came out. McDonald's was an easy, high-profile target, so he went after them to get his name out and rake in the money. Amidst all the controversy this film has generated, the film was generally well-received and was a fun watch. I think, if one choice to eat McDonalds, go for it, despite what this documentary says. There isn't anything wrong, with eating fast-food, as long as you don't overdo it. Too much of anything, can make you sick. If you choose not to eat McDonalds. It's alright, too. Ronald McDonald never pointed a gun to my head and forced me to eat his food. I think this movie did more good for McDonalds than bad, as they reform a lot of their own business practice and often more and more healthy choices. Soon after this documentary was released, McDonald's stopped offering the super-size option for their meals and introduced a 'Go Fit!' meal. They claim that these changes were unrelated to the documentary's popularization, but in my opinion, I think it did. Overall: It's a well-made documentary that worth taking out.</fullReview>
