		<username>t_atzmueller</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>The Legacy of Michael-Moore-like "documentaries"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After Michael Moore's "Bowling for Columbine" had become a major hit and after Moore collected an Oscar with another documentary, the market seemed to brim with similar projects. After all, documentaries like those don't take much to make: An idea, a presenter to convey the idea, a camera and, in case it's needed, the right twist towards the right direction in the editing room. Only, people forgot of major thing: Following the Michael Moore formula takes you from documenting (as in: showing what there is) into the realm of Gonzo-journalism, which contains a kernel of truth, bent into the direction intended by the producer.Next to the Michael Moore films, Spurlock's "Super Size Me" may well have been one of the most successful "Gonzo-Documentaries" that have flooded the market since. This wasn't a film for people who go to fast-food-joints every day, this was a film for people who don't frequently visit places like "McDonald's", "Burger King", "Wendy's" and a zillion others. I must admit: I'm not a big fan either. Sure, once a year, perhaps if I'm on the run or simply in the mood, I'll pay them a visit and order a BigMac with fries (if I recall it correctly, they call them "Quarter-Pounders" in the US). Reminds one of childhood, when I dragged my parents to "McDonald's" because I saw the ads in some comic-book. It's live eating Ramen-noodles in college: Let's call it nostalgia. But time moves on, tastes develop and today I do actually value food, flavor and diversity in what I eat, even if it will cost a buck more. So let's just say that I'm not a hater of fast-food, but that I'm just not a big fan.I must admit too: the movie was entertaining. Almost like watching an episode of "Jackass", where the whole point of watching the show is to see the crew do some incredible stupid stuff and ? aren't we all little sadist to a point? ? getting a little hurt. At first, Spurlock even had me conned. Seeing him degenerate from an average healthy guy to a physical wreck, who would doubt that the rumours and suspicions were true and "MacD"'s products would make people sick? Of course, there was no mention, that Spurlock was a Vegan and hence physically not well equipped to handle this kind of food.In my opinion, documentary should ask questions. Going out with an opinion and use the documentary to confirm this opinion, no matter what the cost, is no longer documentary-filming. It becomes propaganda and that's as true for "Super Size Me" as it is for any works of Michael Moore and similar film-makers. I'd give the film a 5/10 on the entertainment-scale, but the five points I don't give are because it's simply not an honest documentary-film.At the end of the day, the most valuable thing you can take from this film is the advice of a particularly hardcore McDonalds-fan: having spent the better part of his life eating exclusively at McDonalds, he was asked what the secret was to staying as lean and fit as he was. The answer was simple: avoid the fries.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>All Rather Pointless</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It is genuinely interesting, watching Morgan Spurlock attempt to eat nothing but McDonalds. There's something quite perversely satisfying as we see him become repulsed by the eating the same foods all the time, but it fails to make a larger point.Obviously Spurlock's attempting to point out how unhealthy McDonalds is (quelle surprise), but he does so not from an objective stand-point but from one where he has already made his mind up as to how he wishes to portray the company and its product, Michael Moore-style. If it's not clear from his own reporting, then the imposition of rather daft rules demonstrates this perfectly (if they ask him if he wants a larger size he always has to say yes, he always has to eat three full meals a deal, and he must eat every item on the menu). But why is never really made clear. Is it because consumers too stupid to make decisions for themselves? Whatever the reasoning, the scientific method is clearly not for Spurlock.But even if he had conducted a truly scientific study, it still would've pointless. McDonlads, as far as I know, have never encouraged consumers to eat nothing but McDonalds - the very idea is ridiculous. No one is surprised Spurlock puts on weight and quickly becomes terribly unhealthy, and nor should they be.Rather, Super Size Me is simply an attempt to moralise. Spurlock gets on his soap box and essentially says 'McDonlads is evil and you shouldn't eat their stuff. Better yet, McDonlads should be banned, for the good of you all'.People who have always hated McDonlads congratulate him for it while glorying in their own smug condescension at the lack of taste of the proles, McDonalds go into panic mode - on the one hand conducting a PR campaign to argue they're not an evil corporation while with the other they introduce new, healthier ranges of food, and the average consumer proves wiser than the lot of them and carries on having a (proper) McDonalds every once in a while.</fullReview>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>McBrilliant - 93%</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Despite being European and nowhere near as obese as some shown here, I have a horrible feeling that this hard-hitting documentary is designed exactly for people like me. I don't go to fast-food joints that often and never McDonalds because I've never trusted them and don't like their food - give me KFC any day! But like tobacco and alcohol, the dangers of saturated fat, excessive calorie intake and the media's current target of sugar have been talked about for years and the truth is that people don't like being told that they're fat, lazy and/or eating too much junk. The real strength of "Super Size Me" lays not just in presenting an extreme argument against such food and the industry but in how it forces you to look at your own life and reassess your lifestyle.Morgan Spurlock discusses the ever-expanding obesity crisis, focusing on the US in particular and the number one provider of fast food, McDonalds. He decides to ignore the pleas of his vegan girlfriend and indulge in a 30-day diet of eating nothing but McDonalds three times a day, monitoring the effect on his health throughout. During his ordeal, he interviews those involved in the food industry and various regulatory bodies in America who all seem in agreement that McDonalds presents a challenge to the waistlines of ordinary people in a number of ways from persuading children into their restaurants with that damn clown to filling their products with numerous additives to encourage an addiction to their Big Macs. But the effects of Spurlock's crazy experiment take a drastic toll on his mind and body, effects that not even his doctors could have predicted..."Super Size Me" somehow maintains an irreverent tone throughout discussing a serious issue, feeling almost like heavyweight documentary-maker Michael Moore's "Bowling For Columbine". This is no bad thing - humour and satire are powerful tools when presenting an argument and Spurlock's assertion that McDonald's are acting in a morally negligent manner is pretty much irrefutable. Granted, the Golden Arches don't get much in the way of counter-argument as they repeatedly decline Spurlock's attempts at securing an interview with anyone. But there are other issues besides the number of chicken McNuggets one consumes - exercise is quickly disappearing from schools in the US (and what is there simply isn't enough) while adults don't have the time to work off those killer calories. The other problem is choice - how likely are people to choose healthier options when the nutritional information they need is hidden, the advertising budget for fruit and veg is dwarfed by those like McDonald's and Hersey's and even the "healthy" options available are still loaded with sugar? It may feel one-sided and slightly derivative when the film uses cartoons to illustrate certain points (much like Moore does) but "Super Size Me" is essential viewing, if a little graphic at times. It shines a spotlight on an industry that works hard to keep its business obscured in shadow but also the responsibilities of those in Government supposed to protect consumers, swayed by lobbyists on behalf of the same manufacturers as well as the responsibilities of you, the viewer. At the end of the day, only you can decide what to eat and how often and take it from me, I shall be thinking long and hard next time I get the hankering for a Bacon Double Cheeseburger from Burger King. As Spurlock himself asks, who do you want to see go first - you or them? If a documentary was measured on the strength of how it affects you and informs your future decisions then "Super Size Me" is a certified success. Whether it will make me lose weight and eat healthier, I believe I'm too stuck in my ways but at least I'm thinking about it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2013-04-17</date>
		<username>shannondoty87</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Entertaining, Mediocre Experimental Design</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Super Size Me" is an earnest attempt on the part of Morgan Spurlock to increase awareness and educate the public on the harmful implications of eating fast food at frequent intervals. One of the more admirable qualities of his strategy was the fact that this challenge was undertaken at his own personal expense. While this appears to be an honest endeavor when taken at face value, I also found certain design elements of his 30-day experiment to be unrealistic and exaggerated at best. I will give Spurlock credit in regards to his approach in determining his health baseline. He did not seek to be evaluated from just one, but three doctors, a nutritionist, and a personal trainer. The confirmation from these multiple specialists positively impacted the reliability of the tests that detailed his initial health. His evaluation was also comprehensive and pointed to the notion that his health was, indeed, excellent overall. Spurlock's presumably "perfect" health had deteriorated exponentially over the course of his 30-day experiment. He gained over 20 lbs., experienced sexual dysfunction, and his triglyceride levels were elevated. The burning question that arose in me while considering his initial health paired with the outcome of his experiment is "Would most Americans experience similar outcomes?"Spurlock was a cultural anomaly to begin with, eating a clean diet and exercising often. Is it possible that the "typical" American would have responded in a less extreme manner to the experimental conditions than Spurlock? Similar to an alcoholic, for instance, humans have the ability to build a tolerance to substances that are bad for us. If Spurlock lived an exceptionally healthy lifestyle before, barely delving into fast food meals, it is possible that he shocked his system into reacting in such profoundly negative ways. It would have been excellent if the filmmaker had engaged in this experiment counter to a "typical" American who does not normally eat healthy or exercise. I also think that the radical nature of the experimental conditions- 3 McDonalds meals per day, always super sizing when asked, eating each item on the menu at least once- were as such that very few Americans could relate to the outcomes that Spurlock experienced. The experiment seemed to be designed in an effort to polarize his baseline health with the experimental outcome, and, in turn, sensationalize the impact of eating McDonalds. I found this aspect of the film to be quite manipulative. Most Americans eat fast food on a moderate basis, and this is okay, as long as individuals are able to otherwise maintain control over their diets. Overall, Spurlock did a decent job shocking the public into thinking more critically about their fast food consumption. This was perhaps the point. I did think that the film could have been more realistic and applicable to everyday Americans, but, then again, it might not have garnered so much attention from the public had he taken this approach. The primary lesson that one can take from this film is that ingesting any harmful substance, including food, in abnormally excessive amounts over a period of time will negatively affect one's health.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>This movie is a documentary that examines one of the major issues that faces our nation today, obesity. In the documentary Morgan Spurlock eats only fast food from McDonalds for a total of 30 days. There were four simple rules that Mr. Spurlock had to follow such as no options (he could only eat what was available over the counter which included water), no super-sizing unless offered (which happened quite often), no excuses (he had to eat every item on the menu at least once), and no giving up (he had to eat three square meals a day, breakfast, lunch and dinner). Along his journey of fast food he retained doctors to check his health status to keep assessing on how his overall health is progressing. Over the course of his study, he gains over 18 pounds; experiences an extreme increase in cholesterol; suffers sexual dysfunction, headaches, and nausea; almost goes into liver failure; and shows signs of addiction.The one part of the film that really bothered me was when they visit a public school to examine the school lunch program. Watching the students eat a lot of junk food and understanding how school lunches have contributed to the overall unhealthy lifestyle of our children was discouraging and depressing. As adults we must come to realize what these types of foods are doing to our children. We need to take charge of the choices and do the right thing for the children.Super Size Me is a film that covered its bases along with asking difficult questions about American's health. While the film was entertaining it was also an eye opener. I would not suggest this film for younger children due to certain language and other issues that are present in the film. You might think twice about eating fast food again if you see this film.</fullReview>

		<username>arwalter</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>entertaining and educational</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Supersize Me tracks a health-conscious man named Morgan Spurlock as he embarks on a one-month diet of McDonald's food?and only McDonald's food. In the course of the documentary, the viewer sees a man in above- average condition digress as he gains weight, becomes more and more lethargic, and faces dangerous consequences on his liver. Spurlock's point is clear from the beginning: to show how McDonald's and other fast food corporations are contributing to, and even causing, obesity and its numerous related health issues in America. He sets several guidelines, such as trying everything on the menu at least once, only super sizing when asked, and keeping his walking and physical activity at a bare minimum. He follows this last rule by using a pedometer and the reported averages given to him by an expert regarding the average American's number of steps taken per day.Although Morgan shows some bias, he clearly has done his research and strongly appeals to pathos, ethos, and logos throughout the film. It is interesting to watch, with its bright colors, catchy music, and extensive use of charts, maps, and diagrams. The statistics about the prevalence of obesity and diabetes in the U.S. are astounding. The film pairs these facts with images and leaves the viewer feeling sad for these people and outraged that such a huge problem exists, when it could be corrected with just a healthy diet and regular exercise. I was aware of the growing problem obesity is, but these facts still really shocked me. This is something I noticed while watching the film?I "knew" the information already, and the results of Spurlock's experiment were, for the most part, predictable, but sitting and watching almost two hours of it made me realize how much I ignore the problem. Sure, we all know the dangers of fattening foods, but Spurlock stepped up and addressed the issue, and did something about it by letting the world in on his experiment. I appreciated the variety of experts and their opinions throughout the film. Morgan interviewed people on the street, included dialogue with McDonald's employees, filmed his visits with doctors and medical experts, and interviewed people involved in the court cases. The viewer was able to see different points of view and this developed a strong element of ethos for the film. Another contributing factor to this is the fact that Spurlock was ready to try it himself. If he had simply presented the cases and facts without involving himself directly, it would not have been as interesting nor as effective. From throwing up after his first super-sized meal to his doctor's serious concern about the worsening condition of his liver, Spurlock's willingness to test the effects of a McDonald's diet firsthand were commendable. Throughout the movie, the different sections are introduced using a melancholy image of Ronald McDonald and his cartoon friends. Spurlock looks at the ways McDonald's targets children, and the rising obesity and cases of diabetes in children today. From the Happy Meal toys to restaurant playgrounds to television commercials, McDonald's seems to target children more than any other age group. Looking at this aspect enforces Spurlock's message. Most would agree that adults have at least some degree of responsibility when it comes to their eating habits, but many children do not have much choice. We see these innocent kids being convinced that cholesterol-raising cheeseburgers and sugary sodas are components of a "good" meal. In one scene, Spurlock holds up cards for elementary school students to identify. They struggle to name common figures such as presidents and a representation of Jesus, but all correctly identify an image of Ronald McDonald without hesitation. He also takes an interesting look into our country's public school lunch system, revealing another place children are trapped into making poor food choices.For me, one of the most shocking scenes was when Spurlock and his camera crew went to the surgery of a man undergoing gastric bypass surgery. The man shared about his unhealthy diet, including insane amounts of soda. The surgery was captured on film. I think this scene really makes one think about the consequences of eating fast food, and an unhealthy diet in general. If things get so severely out of control, it is difficult to get back on track by exercising and diet change. The fact that people resort to surgeries that actually make their stomachs smaller is mind-boggling. It's enough to make me think twice before choosing fries over carrots.I found this film to be both informational and entertaining. It was fast-paced and kept my attention, while at the same time, involved my emotions, making me feel sympathy for people at times and outraged at the fast food corporations at other times. It's use of a wide variety of techniques, including interviews, charts, statistics, and personal stories added to its credibility and entertainment. I recommend this as a worthwhile film that, if nothing else, will provoke your thoughts and make you think about fast food and health in a new light.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Who's up for a Big Mac? The item of America and of its eating itself to death, here, given a frightening but oddly entertaining exploration.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Morgan Spurlock's Super Size Me felt more-a damning of America's eating habits and over indulgence in fast-food than it did a demonisation of McDonalds. That's not to say McDonalds gets of entirely, it's just the 2004 documentary did not do enough to permanently turn me away from said chain, or indeed any fast food outlet, and I find myself able to frequent one every now and again if needs be. Rather, his film is a statement on, or an exploration of, just how partaking in such diets and eating habits can ruin a person's life just as much as it can put them at great risk linked to one's own health. A case for blaming those whom are the ones consuming mass quantities of the product, and not the product itself, ought not entirely fall on deaf ears; rather, the documentary will go on to further explore just what it is that gives McDonalds that mystery; that aura; that reputation, predominantly in locating just where some of its outlets actually are on top of what it is McDonalds appears to stand for or represent. In short, the documentary is a pretty terrifying exploration into the nutritional unknown; there can be few foods or stonewall diets in the world that would have this kind of an affect on a human, Super Size Me being all of a grotesque; wondrous; off the wall; distasteful; shocking and yet strangely alluring experiment undergone by a charismatic and ridiculously brave soul in Mr. Morgan Spurlock.Spurlock's film explodes into life with a fierce documenting of fast food and the situation regarding fast food in the United States today; an expositional placing down of the facts onto the proverbial table, the informing us on the severe lengths certain companies within the arena of fast food have gone so as to achieve restaurant proximity and a grip on a nation of fast-foodies. After dishing out the bare bones on the situation, the severity of the situation and the results of such severity in the situation; he calmly appears on screen and outlines exactly what it is he'll do in a manner that is not nonchalant, but confident, but with a hint of worry: all the meals he will eat from now on for the period of a month will be from the McDonalds menu, with the mandatory updating to Super Size if the clerk offers such an option. The documentary is the capturing of this experiment; amply supported by girlfriend Alexandra, who's often on the camera, as well as an array of doctors and nutritional experts he calls upon, Spurlock sets out in what we can only perceive as the goal amidst the madness, in the hope of proving McDonalds are to blame for the obesity problem America has.Before we set out on the road, an interview with a person whom tried to sue a tobacco company sees the film attempt to paint a portrait of McDonalds as a company more geared towards children and one that perhaps preys on them. Spurlock opts for content which will go a long way in having us side with him from the off; the proposing of a nation's child generation as those whom are victimised by a company whom offers food on top of other services such as: Happy Meals, play-zones and the ability to play out parties for children. The tactic is designed to play on the audience's emotions and mind, quickly forcing us into constructing an image of the company being rather evil minded in this sense. Shamelessly, Spurlock vomits there and then out of the window of his car the first time he goes for the Super Size option; the feeling that the road ahead will be long and tough coming to be prominent.The journey, of which Spurlock refers to as a "tour of duty", is of a warts-and-all ilk; the getting through all of the food, all of the time, a disgusting enough of a concept to handle on the one hand with the regular visits to clinics for check ups on his grossly, ever-deteriorating health complete with one-stop trip to the operation theatre so as to peek in at one man's surgery a gruelling watch all in itself. Later on, problems arise when it is revealed in a rather personal moment that he is wholly fed up of the menu after a meagre ten days; prompting the question as to how he'll put up with it from then on in. His calling of what he's doing as an aforementioned tour of duty suggests something nationalistic in his actions; as if he is braving the unknown in search of what's right for the American people; indeed, the people of the world ? it would be fair to say he serves the tour well.Spurlock goes where few would even dare to contemplate, and yet the disturbing truth of the matter is that it would appear most people aren't invariably too far away from where Spurlock was during that month in his diet. The film is much more than a mere Jackass stunt; here is an experiment in all its grotesque and grizzly glory, a genuine venturing off into the unknown so as to test a genuine thesis upon which the sorts of content naturally plays out. The piece is delicately inter-cut amidst professional opinion in the form of doctors and nutritionists, with street-set vox pops snippets that offer their own element of comic relief, with Spurlock's own plight as he munches his way through an outrageous amount of Big Macs and Quarter Pounders; each strand both fascinating and curiously humorous in its own right culminating in an accomplished documentary for the ages.</fullReview>

The obvious reason is that the subject and conclusion of the movie finds relevance and resonance with a lot of people concerned about health and looks.Morgan Spurlock, writer, director and main "actor" in the documentary who seems like a health buff whose girlfriend is a vegetarian chef, undertakes to eat nothing but McDonald's fast foods for one month. He puts himself under the care of a physician, a nutritionist and various other medical specialists to record his state of health before the experiment and to monitor the "progress" or rather the deterioration of his health in the duration of his experiment. Not surprisingly, his internist, Dr. Daryl Isaacs, several times in the course of the month-long "adventure" strongly advised him to go off the McDo diet as his cholesterol levels shot up to unhealthy parameters as well as the occurrence of deleterious changes to his pulse, respiration and overall resistance under medically controlled stress tests. He also complained of strange tingling sensations in unmentionable parts of his nether region. As for me being an older person whose sensations in the same parts are waning, I would be hard put to say whether that would be an unwelcome development.As a candidate to become an emergency medical technician (basic), I found the disclosures in the film and the results of Spurlock's experiment alarming. I live in a working class neighborhood where parents (some couples, quite a number - single parents) probably do not have much time to shop fresh food and cook them. McDonald's representatives refused to be interviewed by Spurlock. Nevertheless, after its release, McDonald's came out with allegedly healthy items in its menu including salads (alas still loaded with calorie rich cheeses and dressings) and Mexican style corn wraps.I wonder if there is a correlation between the fact that I often see the blue and white ambulances of my destination rescue station on call in my neighborhood and the fact that many of my neighbors practically subsist on fast foods for their staple diet?</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The story of then unknown filmmaker Morgan Spurlock's odyssey to uncover the effects of a solid fast-food diet,via an entire month of eating nothing but McDonald's,is pretty well documented by now. The darling(in tandem with Michael Moore's scathing and somewhat over-blown Iraq War/anti-Bush administration documentary epic Fahrenheit 9/11)of a number of film festivals,it would be the talk of anyone who had ANY interest in health,food education or simple curiosity about what it really WOULD be like to indulge in the world's most easily identifiable and popular fast food for that long.A smart,well-thought endurance test for anyone who's had some digestive problems(of which I count myself among),this is a cheerfully broad swipe at not just the fast food industry(with small swipes at Baskin Robbins and the Soda companies)but at Supermarket retailers,the corporate strategies behind them,the educational initiatives(or usually the lack thereof),the disparity of food marketing,profits and advertising and--last but not least--social attitudes toward eating. Plenty of editing and graphic,a fair injection of "man on the street" interviews and,of course,Spurlock's own observations,reactions to the diet and pitfalls(which are a bit painful to endure at a point,to be honest)which are matched by the skepticism,horror and disbelief at his experiment shared by his girlfriend(a Vegan chef,no less!),a series of doctors and health consultants and even his mother. Truly a packed show that is largely satisfying.A tad preachy,it still has a delightfully lighter touch than many of Moore's movies(particularly Fahrenheit)and while the social/health-wise impact the show maybe aiming for is somewhat negligible in result(aside from "not making" McDonald's eliminate the Super Size option on their menus not long after this show's theatrical run),it is still a largely entertaining and eye-opening tour de force. Extras on the DVD are somewhat "off the reservation",choosing to deal more in general food/health quandaries(for example:the location of breakfast cereals and candy placement in your average supermarket)than necessarily any direct relation to the fast food questions,but still definitely worth a look.</fullReview>

What's funny is the response of his dietitian and doctors monitoring him, who didn't realize how damaging fast food was to the body. The tragic part was the health center that monitored him during that short month went bankrupt! No money in healthy lifestyles, but plenty in fast food. Spurlock only touches on some big topics like school lunches and the psychology of programming kids to know fast food places through the use of cartoon characters, so he stays on topic of the 30 day experiment which is a good thing. Obesity is such a huge complex issue that you can't really address every aspect in a single documentary. This documentary may not keep you from eating McDonalds forever, but it will make you think twice before eating fast food on a regular basis.</fullReview>

		<date>2010-01-31</date>
		<username>calvinnme</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Interesting film on the state of nutrition in America</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Morgan Spurlock's film is based on an experiment that uses himself as a guinea pig in which he ingests nothing but food and beverages sold by McDonald's for 30 days to see the results. Spurlock is a healthy man in his early 30's who - prior to this film - has eaten a healthy diet, maintained a healthy weight, and gotten plenty of exercise on a regular basis. He is embarking on this experiment because apparently McDonald's has made the claim that you could eat their food daily as part of a healthy diet. Spurlock's rules are he can eat nothing but McDonald's food for 30 days, he must eat three meals a day, and if asked if he wants to supersize something he must always say yes. On top of this, he simultaneously cuts back on his exercise to be something corresponding to what the average American gets. Also, though not part of his official rules, he never orders a salad or bottled water. He always goes right for the greasiest food offered. It should be obvious to anyone that this is not what McDonald's meant in their press release as Spurlock goes on a month long fast food binge. At least part of the results were not that startling to me. If you are over thirty and you do this to yourself, even for just a month, you should expect to gain a lot of weight and feel awful - which he does. He gains about ten percent of his original body weight over the month. What did surprise me was that the diet did so much damage to his internal organs so quickly. Spurlock's blood pressure shot up, he began to show signs of liver damage, and all of his blood tests frightened his doctor so much that he told him to go to the emergency room if he should begin to experience shooting pains in the middle of the night.Actually, Spurlock's experiment doesn't take up probably more than half the film. A large part of the documentary is spent talking about the state of nutrition in America, focusing particularly on the food served in the public schools. He visits several typical public schools in which kids can order anything they want at the cafeteria, have ready access to candy and soda via vending machines in the halls, and have greatly reduced physical education classes due to budget constraints and, although not mentioned, quite probably the greater emphasis on teaching to the standardized tests that students have to take in most states that takes up a great deal of school time. For contrast, Spurlock visits a school in Wisconsin for troubled kids in which organic food is served and notes that the behavior problems in these children is vastly reduced, most likely the result of this diet. This part of the film really made me think. I'm almost fifty, and when I was a child in public school we had to eat a balanced diet if we ordered one from the cafeteria, had limited access to desserts, and had no vending machines. There were no soft drinks served at lunch - only milk, iced tea, and water. Finally, we had daily P.E. classes in which we all had a period of mandatory calisthenics. I also remember that obese children were a rarity, and now if you look around they seem to be everywhere. Spurlock doesn't limit himself to examining the health habits of children, though. He also points out how the portion sizes in restaurants have grown tremendously and so have the waistlines of adults. Spurlock does come across one very interesting human oddity of an exception that proves the rule. This fellow has been eating an average of three Big Macs a day for years, is obviously in at least his late 30's or maybe even 40's, and is as thin as a rail. I'd love to find out if this behavior ever catches up with him.Basically, Spurlock's experiment on himself is used as a kind of Frankenstein horror tale to hold your interest while he talks about the crisis brewing in America from a combination of bad eating habits and greatly reduced physical activity. He doesn't seem to have any answers, though. People have to work longer hours to pay the bills, giving little time for exercise and home-cooked meals, and public education budgets have gotten tighter. The high price of housing has meant people with families live further from the center of cities in order to find affordable homes in safe neighborhoods and thus spend more time in traffic and less time walking. Since Spurlock himself is living the life of a single guy in New York City with no family pressures and can walk to the office every day, he would probably have a difficult job seeing that for many people just recognizing this problem isn't enough to solve it. However, it is a start.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2009-11-04</date>
		<username>zardoz-13</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>An Appetitizing, Anti-Fast Food Documentary That Isn't Entirely Objective</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Documentary films often qualify as freeze-dried exercises in persuasion that masquerades as extended news stories. Freshman filmmaker Morgan Spurlock struck a chord with his 2004 documentary "Super Size Me" about the health hazards of fast food. No, Spurlock was not the first to sound the alarm about fast food. Eric Schlosser's non-fiction book "Fast Food Nation," published in 2001, was the first to investigate the fast food industry. The "Atlantic Monthly" magazine correspondent chronicled the insidious growth of the fast food industry in America. Ultimately, not only did Schlosser argue that America boasts the highest obesity rate of any industrial nation, but he also attributes this obesity to fast food. Mind you, nobody has proved beyond a doubt that fast food paves the way to obesity. Nevertheless, Schlosser writes, "it seems wherever America's fast food chains go, waistlines inevitably start expanding." Indeed, Spurlock seems to have perused Schlosser's book. He spouts similar information and covers the changes in American society that Schlosser document. Once upon a time the mother of the household cooked all the meals in her kitchen that a family ate until fast food began to exert an impact on middle-class America life. Thereafter,families began to dine out. Ironically, Spurlock's documentary about McDonalds surpasses the surprisingly lightweight film adaptation that eventually appeared about "Fast Food Nation." Although the film "Fast Food Nation" contains the basic ideas espoused in the book, the film lacks the personable compelling quality of "Super Size Me." Recently, in his film "Fat Head" (2009), comedian Tom Naughton challenged Spurlock's thesis that fast food is bad food. Naughton's film serves as a rebuttal to Spurlock's "Super Size Me." Whereas Spurlock gained almost twenty-five pounds on a month-long McDonald's diet, Naughton lost 12 pounds on a similar McDonald's diet.The best and the worst about "Super Size Me" can be traced back to writer, producer, and director Morgan Spurlock. Spurlock graduated with a Bachelor of Fine Arts in film from New York University's Tisch School of the Arts in 1993. Before he wrote, produced and directed "Super Size Me," Spurlock penned plays and his play "The Phoenix" won awards at both the New York International Fringe Festival in 1999 and the Route 66 American Playwriting Competition in 2000. He maintains interest throughout his 100 minute documentary chiefly by exploiting himself as a guinea pig for his indictment of fast food in general as well as McDonalds specifically. Spurlock is an affable, often amusing fellow who fearlessly hurls himself into this project and he makes sure that you feel sympathetic toward him. He makes himself seem believable because everything that takes place happens to him. When Spurlock is not addressing the audience, he performs the equivalent of "60 Minutes" interviews that he places as a counterpoint to his own story. Most of the people that Spurlock interviewed have something to do with obesity and medicine so the interviews are pertinent and the authorities interviewed are intelligent. Interspersed throughout Spurlock's story and the interviews are clever graphics. Spurlock serves all this information up with obvious humor. "Super Size Me" is not dead serious. Nevertheless, Spurlock generates some drama about his condition with his girlfriend and his medical authorities, all of whom are concerned that he is doing irreparable harm to his body. Consequently, McDonald's amounts to a villain, but McDonald's executives had nothing to do with the making of "Super Size Me." "Super Size Me" chronicles a 30-day time period (from February to March 2003) when Spurlock ate nothing but McDonald's food. During that period, we watch the effects the dining on McDonald's has on Spurlock's physical and psychological health, while he investigates the fast food industry's corporate influence, primarily how McDonald's and others promote poor nutrition for its own profit. Spurlock ate at McDonald's restaurants three times per day and tried every item on the chain's menu in nine days. Whenever he was given the option to "super-size" a meal, Spurlock agreed to have his order "super-sized." Consequently, he treated himself to the equivalent of 9.26 Big Macs) a day. Eventually, the 32-year-old Spurlock added 24? lbs., a 13% body mass increase, a cholesterol level of 230, and suffered mood swings, sexual dysfunction, and liver damage. Spurlock spent fourteen months losing the weight gained from his experiment."Super Size Me" garnered an Academy Award nomination for best documentary, but it did not win in that category. Nevertheless, the success of "Super Size Me" is that it compelled McDonald's to abandon their campaign to persuade customers to buy more food as a part of their diet, but it also paved the way for Spurlock to produce more documentaries. Spurlock's later documentaries have neither been as successful as "Super Size Me" nor have they been as provocative. Interestingly, when "Super Size Me" came out as a DVD, Spurlock added a brief epilogue about McDonald's decision to drop the Super Size option six weeks after the premiere of the film. Since then McDonalds has created a menu that includes supposedly healthier items on its menu, such as salads, and a new adult happy meal. Unfortunately, these salads pack even more calories than the hamburgers, if the customer adds liberal amounts of cheese and dressing. Naturally, McDonald's claimed that "Super Size Me" did not influence the fast food titan to make these menu alterations."Super Size Me" is amusing, insightful, but not entirely objective documentary.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>dunmore_ego</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Eat It To Death.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Morgan Spurlock tries to find the answer to why the Mcdonalds' corporate, food-like substance has such a stranglehold on our Neanderthal tastebuds, in his diabolically insightful SUPER SIZE ME, a documentary of an experiment on his own innards - to eat nothing but McDonalds' food for thirty days to note its effect on him, physically and psychologically.Spurlock sets Rules for himself. Besides the Zeroth Law of exclusively McDonalds for 30 days, he: #1. will only Super Size when asked. #2. can only eat food from McDonalds, water included. #3. has to eat everything on the menu at least once. #4. must eat three meals a day. But SUPER SIZE ME is not simply a clinical annotating of one man's personable and engaging take on the putrefaction of his innards. It is as informative as it is entertaining. It is as funny as it is indicting. Not only does Spurlock bring in nutritionists, doctors and other professionals to scientifically monitor his health before, during and after his experiment, he also delves into every aspect of junk food in American society. From mega-corporations psychologically entrapping kids from a young age with their clowns, playgrounds and Happy Meals, to the physical addiction brought about by additives and flavorings, to exploring school menus and phys-ed programs. Spurlock eats only of the McDonalds poisons, but all fast food chains and junk food companies are targeted in his research and observations. As expected, the most advertised foods are the most consumed foods. And advertising budgets for the junk and candy industries overwhelm the health food industry budget like a fat chick on a cupcake. He interviews everyone up and down the fast food spectrum, from corporate monkeys who get entrapped in their own duplicity, to lawyers involved in food industry lawsuits, to people on the street, some of whom wouldn't know a treadmill if they fell off one. He even uncovers that no layperson knows what a "calorie" is - amazing, considering we all see it on the sides of food packaging and use it to measure how much of that food we shouldn't eat. Spurlock gets clarification from Marion Nestle PhD MPH (Chair of Nutrition and Food Studies NYC): "A calorie is the measure of the energy content of food... one calorie is the amount of energy that's needed to raise the temperature of a liter of water by one degree centigrade."On Day 2 - he pukes. A third of the way through a Super Sized lunch, Spurlock realizes just how insane the quantity of food is - but for the sake of the experiment, he must force it down. and it comes back up. Unintentionally funny, but proves a grim point: Spurlock is a reasonably healthy guy - his girlfriend Alexandra is a vegetarian cook - and just one day of forcing this radioactive garbage down his throat caused his body to reject it; now think of the large part of the population who routinely ingest this glop, and the words of Kelly Brownell PhD (Eating and Weight Disorders), don't seem far-fetched: "We live in a toxic food environment... that almost guarantees we become sick." Phil Lawler, a phys-ed teacher in Illinois: "We've never had health care in this country. We've only had sick care." (Truth that the pharmaceutical companies in America will never admit to; truth that keeps governments in Big Pharm's pocket 'til death do they part - our death).Luckily, Spurlock likes the food - as we all do, for its chemical induction of our buds - but its effect on his health becomes so adverse that his MD, Daryl Isaacs, advises him off the McDiet for fear of actual death. They never admitted that SUPER SIZE ME influenced or affected their business model, but it is no secret that congruent with this film's release, McDonalds Corp introduced many "health" foods to its menu - salads and various hippie dishes - targeting greenies and vegetarians and worried parents. The sea change didn't just overwhelm McDonalds - the whole fast food industry overhauled its aspect towards "health" and "nutrition." --Review by Poffy The Cucumber (for Poffy's Movie Mania).</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Super Size Me</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 2004, a man named Morgan Spurlock decided to explore the fast food industry's corporate influence on our society's health, fitness, and overall physical well being by conducting an experiment. He came to the decision that he would limit himself to only eating food from McDonald's for thirty days. His inspiration came from a court case where two teenage girls attempted to sue the fast food industry for making them obese. The judges ruled that they did not have enough evidence to prove that the fast food company was to blame, so they lost. Spurlock was motivated to prove that these companies were in face to blame for much of the world's obesity, so project "Supersize Me" began. Spurlock focused on a few specific concepts when tackling this project. He did an excellent job at pointing out the obvious arguments of nutrition, but went further into looking more in depth at the court cases to see if it were even logical to sue them for their obesity. Then he looked at the fast food industry's affects on this world through their advertising, focusing in mostly on children. Finally he researched the addictiveness of fast food, and how it truly can be as harmful as drugs.For more of my review, go to:http://www.appstate.edu/~grimmettct/fys/film_review/film_review.html</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Very Well Rounded Documentary With Unbiased Presentation.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was Going through a lot of the negative reviews on this film, and ALL of the ones i read were keen to focus on maybe only 1 or 2 ideological flaws with the way it was presented and ignore everything else. In truth, going on a sudden McDonald's binge after eating mostly healthy low fat vegetarian style food, physiologically does not prove much. The film contained a lot of very good information, and to all of those who hated it, he provided reliable and concise statistics, unlike propagandists like Michael Moore. It doesn't matter if you think a documentary about poverty is more relevant, or that you think it's obvious that fast food is unhealthy, or that you think it is the parents fault, not McDonald's, or that people should exercise their own free will, or that processed foods help aid impoverished kids. Everyone knows why processed food exists; because it's cheap to make and easy to prepare and the film merely asks the questions, why do we feed ourselves with cheap food and pay good money for people to prepare food within seconds rather than cook a proper meal especially at most schools as shown in the film.What the film does, is explain in depth how corporations like McDonald's function and shows quite convincingly how they are able to root themselves in the minds of people far more successfully than even religion. The film as a whole is an examination of American society and it is not overly biased, as shown by the scene of the 2 fit young black men who say they love McDonald's and that they're fit cause they exercise.This film does not blame McDonald's exclusively, and does a very good job at looking at the entire spectrum of the issue, though it brings serious light on the practices of Macdonalds and all corporations which is a necessary thing. If you criticize this film over just one single direction it pursues, you have no point whatsoever. Anyone who did not like this film simply had no interest in the subject, and therefore has no business commenting on it. Frankly though it is a good film and successful in what it set out to do, I don't consider it to be particularly worth seeing a second time, which is why it's a good documentary but nothing ground breaking, though it is DEFINITELY not inane.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Super Size Me</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>J.G. Moss Freshman Seminar 12-2-08 "Super Size Me""Super Size Me" is a documentary film produced, edited, directed, and staring Morgan Spurlock. The documentary focuses on the impact the fast food industry is making in the health of Americans, the main target in the film, McDonalds. The film starts with an introduction to Mr. Spurlock and a brief description of his lifestyle and then dives in to his plan to eat nothing but McDonalds for thirty days and see its effect on the human body. Shortly afterwards, the viewer is filled with more information on this giant food chain than ever thought imaginable. Then the film captivates the viewer and takes them along for the ride as Morgan nearly kills himself in this bizarre experiment.The documentary immediately captures the audience with its use of bright colors and interesting plot. Morgan Spurlock narrates the entire movie, going against the traditional documentary style of monotones and thus a theatrical qualude. The controversial aspect of the film is also a major component in grasping the attention of the viewer. Calling out one of the largest international corporations in the world as being a justified killer is not something seen in the movies every day.The audio is one part of this film which I particularly found very effective. There were a few songs that were recognizable from the top of the charts, but the majority of the film was inlaid with very strange but catchy music that pertained to the subject. I had never before heard a song focusing on the eating habits of Americans or the weight gain attributed to eating McDonalds and other fast foods. Having no information on the origin of some of these soundtracks, I wouldn't be surprised to discover many of the songs in the films were created specifically for this documentary.The primary style of filming used in the documentary are interview and traditional third party perspective showing some of the elements of the filming in the final edit. The interviewing portions of the film are intended to be either informative or to show the somewhat harsh reality of the world we live in through some unexpected reactions and statements made. Shooting in a third party perspective allowed the viewer to get a sense that the film was real rather than some fabrication intended to persuade the masses. Towards the end of the film Morgan begins to have serious health issues and the decision of whether or not to continue filming is included in the final production so as to add to this level of reality.Overall the film was very entertaining while at the same time extremely informative. I had heard a great deal of positive criticism prior to my viewing of the documentary, and I must admit that the film far exceeded my already high expectations.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Super Size Me is a great movie.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Documentary Review I had heard a lot about the documentary film Super Size Me, but I was not prepared for what I saw when I actually sat down to watch this film. I had no idea what impact eating McDonalds for 30 days would have on the Producer/Director/Star Morgan Spurlock.This documentary is focused on the unhealthy and addictive characteristics of the McDonalds fast food restaurant, and any other fast food restaurant in general. Morgan Spurlock is the individual who decided to put his body through 30 days of eating nothing but McDonalds' food for Breakfast, Lunch, and Dinner. Within the first day or two of his experiment, he already threw up his Super Size cheeseburger, fries, and soda. I forgot to mention the fact that whenever the employee asks if he would like to Super Size the meal, he had to say "yes." Spurlock had a team of doctors monitoring his body throughout the experiment; before he started, the doctors took blood samples and did complete body examinations. Spurlock was above average for his age in physical fitness, and he had no health concerns whatsoever. However, throughout his new diet, his liver gradually started to turn to fat, and he was at a serious health risk. All of his doctors advised Spurlock to quit his McDonald's diet, but he prevailed; he even became addicted to the greasy, fatty food, which made it even harder to stop eating it. It took Spurlock 14 months to lose the 25 pounds he gained during this experiment, but he proved his point in saying that fast food is definitely not healthy food.Spurlock was a very creative director because he took a lot of different elements and molded them together to create a great documentary. I loved how the movie starts out with a listing of statistics that are accompanied by witty cartoon drawings. It adds something different to the film rather than just showing Spurlock eating the McDonalds' meals. I also loved how he filmed people on the street, asking them how often they eat fast food and what they thought a calorie is. However, I did think that the types of people Spurlock interviewed added an element of bias. Most of the people Spurlock interviewed about fast food and how often they eat it were overweight people. He might have done it on purpose so he could further underline his point of how eating fast food causes bad health, but he should have interviewed a more different variety of people.I also liked the way he shot the film. It was shot in a way that the viewer could feel like they were right there with him; they showed the doctors talking from Spurlock's point-of-view, and then we see Spurlock from the doctor's point-of-view. When Spurlock is in his car ordering McDonalds, the viewer feels as if they are right there in the passenger's seat. He also used a great camera angle on the 28th day of eating McDonalds; he angled the camera to where it was focused on the food as he walked up the stairs of his apartment building. He was commenting on how it was so hard to walk up the stairs, and the fact that the camera was focused on the McDonalds meal during this slyly hints that McDonalds is the cause.The other great thing that Spurlock did was to include interviews with experts and other people who understand the fact that America is going through a health crisis. He talked with the former Surgeon General, the heir to the Baskin Robbins Company, lawyers suing fast food corporations, and many others. These interviews were great because the viewer got to see other facts and opinions from other people who have spent their whole lives studying this topic. I personally enjoyed hearing from the son of one of the founders of Baskin Robbins; he saw his father and uncle have serious health issues due to the fact that they ate ice cream so much. He also suffered from the effects of eating too much unhealthy food, so he could talk about what he felt, which further underlined Spurlock's main point. Another great element added to this film was the use of music. If you listened to the words in the songs chosen, they seemed to go along great with whatever image was being shown on the screen. This adds fun and more entertainment to the documentary.This film does not relate to his other film; it is of an entirely different topic. His other film in entitled Where in the World is Osama Bin Laden, and he received mostly negative reviews from it. This documentary dealt with terrorism, and he viewed it from both sides, which is most likely the reason why is got such negative reviews. He also has a television show called 30 Days; in this show, Spurlock switches lives with someone for 30 days. The 30 days theme is also apparent in Super Size Me, which is probably where he got his inspiration from to do the television show.Spurlock did a great job with this documentary, and he really got his point across to America, and the many fast food restaurants we are so well known for.</fullReview>

(The consequences are listed in a nutshell through illustrations covering a McDonald's.) But the most compelling part for me is when an overweight teenage girl laments about her self-esteem, for she would never live up to the photos of thin (often bare-bellied) women and young adults, whose photo clippings conceal her as she complains about her appearance.Although there is some profanity and some sacrilegious themes involved, this is a documentary worth whether you're a parent of an obese child, a children's health advocate like me, or a PE coach. There is also a Educationally Enhanced DVD edition if you want a cleaner version of the film. This is best viewed with a portioned amount of air-popped popcorn or crudits with hummus on hand or even better - while exercising on a bike or treadmill.This deserves a decent 9/10 stars, just for the PG-13ness of the film's nature.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Great documentary, showing us a truth we wish wasn't real</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"McDonald's, McDonald's, Kentucky Fried Chicken and a Pizza Hut!" These are the lyrics kids are learning now, and they believe these are good restaurants to eat. This is one of the interesting facts that Morgan Spurlock presents in his documentary. The movie Super Size Me, produced and directed by Spurlock, is an award-winning documentary, including an Oscar nomination that shows viewers, by direct example, how fast-food affects the body. The documentary was inspired by a lawsuit against McDonald's, stating that two girls became overweight, according to medical standards, for eating at McDonald's. The lawsuit got dismissed because they could not prove to the judges that McDonald's made them overweight. So Morgan Spurlock decided to prove people the effect that eating at McDonald's, specifically, can have. He commits himself to a McDiet, eating only McDonald's for an entire month. Eating three square meals a day, trying everything on the menu at least once, and always super sizing if offered were the basic rules of the McDiet.Spurlock not only directed and produced, but also is its main character. He tries out this unprecedented experiment which keeps the audience more interested in how it is going to end. The film communicates convincingly well to the audience by showing real-life scenarios, like Spurlock's following the McDiet and visiting doctors to measure the changes his body makes during those thirty days. He also graphically emphasizes the negative effects of fast-food on the body and mind by throwing up, showing depression when not eating and then becoming energetic when consuming McDonald's. Spurlock also visits different cities, including Houston, where there is the most overweight people in America, to see personally the differences in the menus and the super sizing offers that are made to him at McDonald's. The style on the documentary is very effective, keeping the audience paying attention to effects on health that makes them realize how much money is spent on fast-food, and all the money spent on advertisements for unhealthful food against what is spent for the healthy ones. The average distance that a person walks in America is calculated, to make the documentary more convincing and showing realistic statistics.Something that can make people believe that we should not pay attention to this documentary is the question that Spurlock asks himself: Who eats at McDonald's three times a day every day? However, Spurlock explains that a high number of Americans eat at fast-food restaurants two or even three times a day, usually five days a week.The end of the experiment is very impacting by the numbers that the doctors he visits show, including the weight he gained, the rise in his cholesterol, and the damage done to internal organs of his body. The results do not seem manipulated because of the control of the doctors because they provide an objective view of the damage, which allows Spurlock to show the reality of the food America is consuming. Super Size Me allows American consumers of fast-food to realize the effects of the meals Americans choose to eat. Morgan Spurlock brings us 96 minutes of a truth that we have to face and be conscientious about changing.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Gastronomic murder at its best?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Not since Kurt and Courtney (1998) have I enjoyed a documentary so much.Everybody who likes junk food should see this. Heck, the whole world should see it anyway? It came from a disarmingly simple idea (and one that I wish I'd thought of): Morgan Spurlock had heard often that junk food was bad for you. Okay, why not put that premise to the test, and eat only Macdonalds food for a whole month. Thirty days. Seven hundred and twenty hours, or forty-three thousand two hundred minutes...Can you imagine that doing that? Not me ? I gave up eating at all ? yes, all ? fast food outlets in April 1977. And, I knew that if I started eating that poo food again, I'd be sick in a day and probably near death or dead already in a month. So, I take my hat off the Morgan Spurlock for going beyond the call of duty to prove, once and for all, that the type of fast food that most people eat will, in many cases, result in premature death.Look, on only the second day into his food fest, Morgan finished his Super Sized meal and then promptly vomits out the window of his car. Ohhhhh, YUCK! And all in glorious Technicolor or whatever they call it these days. In twelve days, Morgan adds seventeen pounds to his weight ? and his nutritionist, who's monitoring his downfall, begs him to stop drinking all that soda, for Pete's sake. By day 18, his health is definitely in grave decline and his GP also begs him to stop ? STOP ? this punishment to his body. On day 21, Morgan wakes at 2 a.m., unable to breath and with heart palpitations and further blood tests show serious deterioration in his liver, with a cholesterol count off the scale, almost. Hey, this Morgan guy is beginning to look like he should be awarded a Medal of Honour? By day 30, Morgan's put on nearly 25 pounds, and has eaten the same amount of junk food that the average person eats in EIGHT years! No wonder the poor guy felt sick...And, so will you, when you see it. Because you're gonna see it, right? And, after you do, you'll vow never to set foot inside one of those slaughterhouses again, will you? The film does a good job is showing how the food industry lobbies government to push fast food onto the kiddies; and, of course, the media comes for a lot of flak also, and rightly so. Even more interesting for me is that I know this version I saw had been seriously cut by the TV station I caught it on. Why? Well, in the DVD I saw two years ago, there was month long test of MacDonalds fries (which proved that they have zero nutritional value) in the film; and Morgan also met the most obese staff in USA at a particular MacDonalds (in San Diego, I think, but don't quote me). Neither of those events were in this TV version which just goes to show again how much influence advertisers have over the media.Having said all that, this is a fun film, despite the occasional coarse language (which you probably don't want your kiddies to hear) and graphic medical procedures (which the kiddies might find funny anyway). But, overall, it's a stark indictment of the power of business and government working together to promote ill-health and reap obscene profits...and with no end in sight.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-02-04</date>
		<username>Max_cinefilo89</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Entertaining and enlightening, although Morgan Spurlock is no Michael Moore</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 2002, Michael Moore directed the Oscar-winning Bowling a Columbine, an exhilarating and at the same time terrifying reflection on America's relationship with firearms. Two years later, Morgan Spurlock came up with Super Size Me, which analyzes another US "problem": fast food. What makes the two movies so different is the angle from which the directors approached the topics: while Moore relied on interviews, archive footage and his own irony to convey a powerful message, Spurlock decided to try something new.Having read of a lawsuit filed against McDonald's, which got accused of causing two teenage girls'obesity, Spurlock ignored the protests of his vegan girlfriend and thought it might be a good idea to personally verify if fast food is unhealthy. After some meticulous health controls and other preparations, he unveiled his plan: for thirty days he would eat only at McDonald's. Since some people have all their meals there, why shouldn't he? All he would have to do was follow three rules:1. He could only pick the Super Size option if asked to;2. He could only eat and drink McDonald's products, including the water;3. He had to eat every single item on the menu at least once.The film chronicles Spurlock's painstaking journey as he travels through the USA and keeps eating hamburgers and French fries (most of the time in the car, so that people won't complain about his crew filming him). As the days pass, his "diet" starts to affect him seriously, causing doctors and friends to worry. Regardless of their concerns, he decides to continue until the end.Knowing two hours of just him getting fat would have been boring, Spurlock wisely inter-cuts the various stages of his physical decay with context-giving interviews, in which experts give their opinions on why fast food is so popular and how it keeps seducing people despite the fact that its negative effects are publicly known. These inter-cuts are insightful, but they also show Spurlock's main flaw: unlike the more experienced Moore, he doesn't know how to use his material to entertain and simultaneously shock the audience. Some bits are very funny, some are quite upsetting, but there isn't one scene that will make you laugh and wince in the same moment. Spurlock also seriously challenges the viewer's patience (and stomach) by showing some pretty graphic surgery footage involving a man so obese an operation was the only way to save his life. That part would work in a Cronenberg movie, whereas its presence in this film is less justifiable.Nonetheless, Super Size Me is a good, thought-provoking film. It lacks the emotional power of similarly themed documentaries, but the director's "masochistic" film-making style makes it worth a look.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Entertaining if a bit flatulent</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A funny documentary about the dangers of fast-food and the obesity for which it could be to blame.This isn't the specifically anti-McDonalds documentary you might have been expecting. Instead, Spurlock wants to make a film about people's culturally ingrained dependence on (corporately exploited) fast food. He talks about obesity and its contributing factors (diet, lifestyle). Then, sensibly he homes in on one of these and using the stunt of eating nothing but McDonald's for a month sets himself up as the posteriori guinea pig to investigate other peoples' assumptions.Rather like Michael Moore's Bowling for Columbine, things get a bit detached though. The diet and the documentary proper fail to complement one another, i.e. his McDonalds consumption doesn't really confront the points he's trying to make. To this end he uses a sort of chapter system with evil-clown cartoon idents to introduce areas of discussion with which the diet has some relevance. Really, one needs more input from the people like those said to have inspired the documentary (i.e. those suing the company for their ill-health) and, of course, to speak to more - to some! - people from McDonalds (a section of the film where he calls someone more than a dozen times doesn't have any grip).HOWEVER... it would be wrong to dismiss the honesty, good humour and earnest intent of Spurlock's stunt. It all makes for good natured, informative viewing, although the (misappropriating lobbyist's) idea that it will bring companies to book or change viewers' diets is wishful thinking. 5/10</fullReview>

In this entertaining film, the slightly irritating Morgan Supurlock takes the trend in modern eating habits to its logical conclusion, and eats nothing but McDonalds for a month. Terrifyingly, he gains over a stone and a half in weight in this relatively tiny period. Against this personal story, he challenges the huge amounts of money spent pushing fat and sugar laden foods on the consumer, although the delicate link between social class, diet and body image is not addressed (Michael Moore, for example, might have made more of how a health-conscious executive class makes millions by ruining the diet of the poor). Personally, I still think beef is good for you, in moderate quantities. But the film makes one question the gastro-political consensus in favour of big food. And following its cult success, how greatly must McDonalds regret passing on Spurlock's numerous invitations to put their side of the story.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>RAISING THE awareness OF THE POST MODERN unconscious CONSUMER</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The docufilm,Supersize Me exposes what global capitalism is about, which is in this film epitomised by the unconscious consumer. To further explain, Supersize Me illustrates that the consumer has an unconscious awareness of what is occurring, being disinclined to question how their food is produced, and for what ultimate purpose, and for whose primary benefit. In this context, we see this as an extrapolation of Marx idea that the proletariat is alienated from the means of production. Whereas Marx assumed there would be a natural arousal of conscience, some of his revisionists suggest this can only be achieved by educated intellectuals raising the proletariat's awareness of their condition. Here, the film's director endeavours to achieve this task by exposing the affects upon our health fast food has. And by so doing, he manages to highlight what is in many peoples' opinion consumers who are leading worthless, poor quality lives. The poor quality of lives is illustrated in the film by the cheap toxic matter we consumers are willing to consume at the expense of our health for the fast food restaurateur's profit.The fast food restaurant chain's food, which came under scrutiny by the director abolished its 'super sized' portions during the post film period. By doing this, and by emphasising, and focusing on the super sized portions of food in the film, permitted the fast food chain to abolish this choice from its menu. By doing this, the consumer was potentially being misled into thinking it was only the super sized portions of food which were unhealthy, rather than the food they consume in general.Our inclination to eat fast food, over healthy food, it seemed that, according to the director of the film, was caused by the emancipation of women, no longer willing to be subordinate, and enslaved in the kitchen, like his mother was, and partner is! Meanwhile, during the post film period, it has been reported in the media that there is a decline in fast food consumption. When applied this could constitute the end of global style capitalism at least in its current unchecked state.For exposing us to our ignorance, in a commercial format that is accessible to consumers, then this documentary style film deserves recognition.</fullReview>

		<username>Tom Clarke (tjcclarke@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Pointless but Entertaining</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was travelling through America just as this movie came out on DVD and the improbably named Morgan Spurlock was touting his wares all over the place. On the promotional material French-fries (or, to give them their correct name, "chips") were crammed into his smug, opportunistic handlebar-moustache-surrounded gob, and all indications were that this was merely another leftie having a pop at the big corporations in a bid to make a name for himself.A month eating nothing but McDonalds? Pah. Gimmicky nonsense, I thought.Over a year later I actually sat down and watched the thing, and I'm big enough to admit I was only partly right. It IS gimmicky nonsense, but there is an undeniable charm to Super-Size Me that I never gave it credit for.First the nonsense: It is supposedly a damning indictment of American fast food and the grotesque effect it is having on an increasingly obese and inert population. OK, it won some awards, but it strikes me that movie academies are now handing out Golden Globes and Oscar nominations for stating the obvious.Of course eating nothing but burgers and fries and fillet-O-fish is going to leave you bloated, pallid and unhealthy. Do the same thing at Claridges ? a month's worth of gorging on foie gras, Beef Wellington, cr?me brule and all the trimmings - and you're going to feel pretty soporific and have a distinctly Stilton-y taste in your mouth.Spend a month drinking nothing but Jack Daniel's and you're likely to wind up dead. But no-one's going to win any awards for dragging round a film crew while they swig relentlessly from a brown paper bag, belch loudly and aim wayward punches at passers-by (otherwise Judy Finnegan would have a stack of statuettes to balance out the empties).Actually sitting down and watching Super-Size Me has not caused me to change my opinion on the futility of Spurlock's experiment, but it is at least an entertaining ride. There are splendidly jovial pokes at twitchy authority figures and ingeniously selected vox pops with crazed Big Mac addicts and porky consumers.Most importantly for the success of the documentary, Spurlock himself is disarmingly engaging. He employs an unashamedly Michael Moore approach but does not have the same swagger or self-importance. And though his findings do lead to negative conclusions, he does not overtly condemn McDonalds, just urges caution and moderation.The physical evidence is pretty compelling ? Spurlock put on two stone and his GP labelled him a medical phenomenon ? his liver was on the kind of fast-track to oblivion usually reserved for career alcoholics and his cholesterol and body mass index sky-rocketed.Predictably he spends a fair amount of time forecasting doom and gloom for a nation of inactive children addicted to fast food, but for an advocate of healthy school dinners he comes across as considerably less punchable than our own Jamie Oliver.A hint to his motivation for this film could probably be found in Spurlock's fearsome girlfriend, who just happens to be a Vegan chef. At one juncture she quizzes him over his affection for meat and suggests that ham is as addictive as heroin. Maybe stuffing his chops with chicken McNuggets and double quarter-pounders was just a cunning way of avoiding her lentil pie.7/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Balanced, informative and surprisingly entertaining documentary.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I expected this to be about and hour and a half of "I hate McDonald's, so everyone should hate McDonald's". But I was, happily, wrong. Morgan Spurlock did a good job of showing that the fast food industry is not to blame for our nation's obesity problem (at least not entirely, or even predominately). We are to blame for the decisions we make, including but not limited to the type and amount of food we eat (and the amount of exercise we do, or do not, get). He also draws out the facts about how we can do better with our diets and general eating habits. I was mostly surprised that I was not bored to tears during this movie, as Mr. Spurlock actually manages to infuse some humor to the movie (everyone in the theater with me laughed and gasped at all the right moments). I saw this movie while in L.A. Too bad most of the rest of the country will have to hunt down a theater willing to show up the nation's powerful industries (that don't want you to see this movie) and play the film.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2004-06-21</date>
		<username>CelluloiDiva</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Should be required viewing!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spurlock's zany self-dare, to spend an entire month eating nothing but McDonald's food, is a wild ride into a place where the United States fears to tread: the realm of personal accountability and consequence. Spurlock effectively sacrifices his own good health for the enlightenment of the masses, but the masses are reluctant to receive the message.There is indeed something diabolical about the notion that Country Time Lemonade or Gatorade are viable "healthier" alternatives to Cokes and Mountain Dews -- especially when found in a school lunchroom! Sugar is sugar is sugar, and Spurlock averaged a consumption of a pound of the stuff a day during his month-long experiment.We know better, perhaps, but taking action is for the "other guy". Not only does Spurlock highlight the effect of fast food on the American waistline, but its pervasiveness throughout American society. Our demand to "have it our way" in a matter of minutes may save us time, but what does it cost us in the end? Immediate gratification is a harsh master and once in its clutches, it is very difficult to break free. We see this reflected in the marketing of everything from burgers and fries to self-propelled vacuums to bleach-infused towelettes to the latest in disposable razors. And it is especially prevalent in the advertisement, distribution and promotion of weight-loss aids, programs and devices: Slim-Fast, 6-Second Abs, 20 Minutes a Day, 3 Days a Week. It is stunning to see how many people fall prey to these come ons of easy weight loss, but all the more amazing when compared to the reality of Spurlock's own experience: it took him nearly a year to lose all of the nearly 30 pounds he gained IN ONE MONTH!! "Super Size Me" begins as it ends, highlighting the lawsuit brought against McDonald's by two obese teenagers. In the end, the lawsuit is dismissed as frivolous and thrown out of court. No doubt, some lawyer will try another approach to the same issue (love the scene where the girls' attorney, when asked for the motivation for his case, sputters and stammers, finally saying "You mean besides huge financial gain?" before falling into a puzzled silence), seeking the success of the tobacco company suits. But Spurlock has built a case for personal accountability that cannot be ignored. However, providing information is not enough, in and of itself. As demonstrated with his visits to school lunchrooms, that education MUST be coupled with appropriate opportunity to make the best choices. It is not enough to tell children to make smart food choices then provide them with little but pizza, fries, burgers, chips and other junk at lunchtime. Like smoking, we all know that eating junk food will give us junk bodies, but we still persist in the behavior. In time, perhaps the only thing that will motivate us to change will be the same motivation that has brought big tobacco to its knees: money. When it starts costing the taxpayers too much to keep providing bypasses to fatties on the federal dime, then we will see change.</fullReview>
	</review>
