The place where fast food originated is now having to deal with the end result of it's overwhelming success. Large servings (witness Spurlock's first attempt to eat an entire super-sized meal), high calories, and toddler-up marketing may make for a successful business model, but at what price? Obesity is now an issue of the times and Spurlock is among the first to bring awareness of the situation to the masses.</fullReview>

		<username>RobinSisson</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>DON'T MISS this film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I love this film! Morgan Spurlock is this brilliantly average guy who's message is conveyed all the more powerfully for his very averageness. This is a guy I'd date -- and have, in one form or another; he could be your brother or best friend or best friend's brother. He undertakes the interesting experiment of discovering how a month-long McDonald's diet will affect his constitution. His findings, under the administration of a small team of doctors, nutritionists, and health professionals, are astounding; stopping just short of terrifying, really. The very direct and clearly unquestionable effects of his consumption are quick and quite without relent for the duration of the diet -- compared at more points than one in the film, by surprised health care professionals on all levels, to the effects of long-term alcoholism.A really disturbing sub-text of the film is the impact this 'instant gratification' societal psyche we've engendered is affecting our children: even in their schools, who we so often blindly trust to 'teach the children well', the junk food culture abounds. The 'lunchlady' fare of Salisbury steak, reconstituted mashed potatoes, and fruit cocktail that we so frequently suffered as children ourselves has not been subsidized, but replaced in many schools by fries, pizza, chips and what's offered as a 'healthy alternative' to Cokes (by specific example in this film), Country Time Lemonade and Gatorade. The film also explores, in brief, the impact of this diet on our children's performance in school. Particularly as a mother, I found this exploration disturbing, if not entirely unsurprising.Overall, the film is just amazing. It imparts a very important and enlightening message while managing to completely avoid preachiness. Witty, bright, and very easily identifiable; this film shows us, with indubitable and progressive evidence, what we do to ourselves in the short- and long-term, in continually surrendering our bodies AND OUR CHILDREN to the marketing ploys of processed foods...fast food, in particular. This is a DON'T MISS film for one and all.</fullReview>

		<username>noralee</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Original, Entertaining Gonzo Journalism</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Super Size Me" is a very entertaining gonzo journalism documentary.Inserting himself very personally in the tradition of Michael Moore and Hunter Thompson, Morgan Spurlock still manages to cover plenty of carefully researched, objective, serious material while keeping the extensive statistical information visually interesting with charming graphics, including clever animation. I especially liked his original surveys of nutritionists and comparisons of different age groups' familiarity with various U.S. icons vs. McDonald's advertising.While his attempts to get an official response from McDonald's recalls "Roger and Me" for Moore's efforts to get an interview with the head of General Motors, the audience gets really involved in the suspense of the condition of his health over his carefully documented 30 days of eating McDonald's meals, so it was a relief to see him looking thin and healthy on "The Daily Show" this week. The DVD should be packaged with a copy of "Fast Food Nation: The Dark Side of the All-American Meal" by Eric Schlosser, which provides a wider perspective on the same issue and only occasionally covers some of the same points. Spurlock is original in taking on the PC-issues around obesity and drawing many parallels with the tobacco industry and smokers -- do fat people bring this on themselves or are they helpless victims of relentless marketing?</fullReview>

He did the same thing to himself in 30 days that me &amp; my family did in 30+ years. Thank you Morgan Spurlock, for potentially trying to save our lives.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Documentary? No, more like Science Fiction (or Fiction-Science)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>To be fair, I will say that like a Michael Moore propaganda film, I was not planning on wasting my time with this blatant cry for help. However, from an exterior point, I will point out the facts, both those given, and those conveniently neglected to "prove his point". First, let's look at the qualifications. Documentary? Semi. More of a Mockumentary in the spirit of This Is Spinal Tap. Honest? Only when it supported his claims. Let's examine the premise - He's going to spend 30 days only eating food served at McDonalds. Additionally, he must sample at least each item on the menu. Neither premise is feasible in the real world, as both the opportunity for three feedings at this establishment is not practical, and the menu selection stipulation is unrealistic (who does that, other than someone on a challenge?). Next, we'll examine the "scientific nature" of this "study". Is the premise sound? Not really. Is the study neutral or biased? Extremely biased. How was the study conducted? An alleged group of "doctors" and "nutritionists", and a token "vegan girlfriend" which more sounds like the cast list of a so-called "reality show" than a scientific experiment. Further, focus was on one man's binge eating, rather than on any aspect of his life outside of eating. The Results? Representative? No - only one subject used. Quantifiable? No, only one subject used. Repeatable? Questionable, most likely repeat results would depend on the agenda of the next study. Lacking in this movie to give credibility were the following pieces of information. What was this gentleman's diet, exercise level, and health prior to this binge? What was his activity level and exercise regiment during the "study"? Likewise, what were they after his 30 day binge? These details were neglected. Was that because they would not support his result, or because they were never considered? Without them, his claims lack a scientific base. Why was there only one subject? If this was a true scientific and reproducible experiment, then more specimens to prove their findings would help their cause. Why was he intentionally choosing the most unhealthy habits for eating, and portraying them as normal? While I won't say that everyone "eats well" especially at fast food establishments, the exaggeration here is clearly to support an agenda, not to be representative. A similar study performed by actual doctors and nutritionists, could be performed with different results were binging eliminated, and a balance was established, both in diet and exercise. It would also be curious as to how such a "study" would compare when including other fast food establishments. I won't discourage people from seeing this film, but rather encourage them to view it with an open mind. Realize that this is but ONE possible outcome. Remember many facts have been withheld from this film. Remember that "super sizing" is merely a choice that the establishment offers, it is not an obligation. The joy of life in America - you have the choice of where to eat, and once there, what to eat. Mr Spurlock chose to eat at McDonalds for a month. He chose what he would binge on during that time. Is he surprised at the results? Ultimately it's his fault, not McDonalds. He chose poorly. Why should someone else be held to blame for his actions? Keep an open mind. Question what doesn't make sense. Look at the "facts" those there, and those neglected, then make up your own mind.</fullReview>

Smokers all around the nation have been heckled and shunned from public buildings at the risk of disturbing and harming others around them because of the damage that smoking can cause and its long term effects. Fast food (unhealthy foods, fattening foods, etc.) is another epidemic example across the nation that is very similar to smoking. Fast food creates health risks and increases probability of heart failure, or liver failure. Luckily for this new fat epidemic is that the effects of these foods are VISIBLE, unlike smoking. 		<username>Dilip Barman (barman@jhu.edu)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Interesting documentary about the epidemic of obesity in America and what role fast food restaurants play - interesting film that is likely to "preach to the choir"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I just saw "Super Size Me" in the theatre. This documentary film is Morgan Spurlock's quest to show how obese people in the United States are becoming, and how, next to smoking and catching up quickly, it is the second most preventable cause of mortality in the country. In particular, he points a strong finger at fast food companies, especially McDonald's, which he shows lures young people into eating unhealthy food and which doesn't typically offer information on nutritional profiles of their food.Under medical supervision, Spurlock undertakes a month of eating nothing but McDonald's food and saying "yes" whenever offered to "supersize" his meal (I had not heard this term before, but it means buying a larger portion). Pronounced initially as very healthy, he suffers significantly as the experiment proceeds, and he experiences signs of food addiction with energy as he eats followed by often depression and low energy.I think he could have made his point without sticking to the diet for a whole month. As a vegan, I had difficulty watching the scenes of his eating. It was interesting that his girlfriend, Alexandra Jamieson, is a vegan chef - and clearly the month with her boyfriend's becoming "Burger Boy" as some of the medical staff called him was difficult on her.I find it difficult to understand how some people regularly eat at fast food restaurants (Spurlock claims that fast food restaurants call "Heavy Users" those customers who eat at their establishments weekly, and "Very Heavy Users" who patronize them daily!!) or drink sugar-laden carbonated beverages regularly, especially when (I hope!) the unhealthiness is clear. So seeing such behavior in the documentary was sad to me, and colored my view of the film. I wish that he had given some background on the fascinating mislabel case, where activists sued McDonald's in the United Kingdom ( I also would have enjoyed a more substantive addressing of the role of personal responsibility in making food choices.I did like the fact that he had interviews in the film with people like former (1998-2002) US Surgeon General David Satcher, Neal Barnard of Physicians Committee for Responsible Medicine, physicians, and nutritionists, and other professionals who weighed in heavily against eating at fast food restaurants in combatting this obesity epidemic. I also was pleased that he showed the poor job society has done in promoting healthy eating by describing how poor diets are promoted by school lunch programs, even those with federal food subsidies, and how trivial amount of funding goes to promote fruit and vegetables as compared to massive advertising for caloric "junk food". He gave a great example of a Wisconsin school for "at risk" teens, and how they largely turned behavioral problems around by serving good lunches with significant whole-food ingredients, by partnering with a company called Natural Ovens Bakery.The film was cut in a fashion to mirror a feeding frenzy at "fast food", and rhythm was maintained by return to a style of music (perhaps the same song) and a "Day xx" caption at various points when he would cut from exploring some aspect of fast food or obesity and return to a day in his food routine.His use of weaving these elements in to the film formed an effective and interesting story.I'm afraid that this film is likely to appeal to those who are already concerned about public health issues of poor diet and that those who frequent fast food establishments are not likely to be in the audience. And even if unhealthy eaters do watch, the light tone of the film can leave them with a few laughs and perhaps not a gentle push to evaluate their own food choices. Be that as it may, it was a rather unique and interesting film to see.--Dilip Barman, June 7, 2004</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>anhedonia</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Fascinating, funny and frightening</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(WARNING: THIS REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS)A film about a man going on a month long Mickey D's binge could very easily have turned into a monotonous, tedious affair. But not "Super Size Me." Morgan Spurlock's documentary turns out to be at times hilarious, occasionally moving and often frightening.This film should be compulsory viewing in every school in this nation. From its opening - the credits roll to the rocking sounds of "Fat Bottomed Girls" - the film remains well-paced and funny. What's terrifying is how much damage Spurlock does to his body on this ill-advised diet and the realization his three doctors come to that they never thought this much damage could be done by eating fast food. It's akin to damage caused by binge drinking, one observes.Spurlock's cholesterol skyrockets, his blood pressure jumps, he gains 17 lbs. in just the first 12 days, his sex life suffers (his vegan chef girlfriend talks of his lowered sexual appetite in a funny scene) and his liver faces toxic shock. By the end, he's gained almost 25 lbs. and then another shocker - that it took Spurlock nine months to lose all the weight he gained in just 30 days!Here's a man who clearly is harming himself to prove a point to Americans. The moment where his physicians advise him to stop this ridiculous diet with one week still left and the phone call with his girlfriend that follows drive home the danger of this whole enterprise.Spurlock makes it clear that personal responsibility plays a huge role in one's diet. But he also makes a persuasive case about how attractive fast-food restaurants make their food for children. And that's the really frightening part about "Super Size Me" - a nation of children who can identify a picture of Ronald McDonald easier than, say, Jesus Christ.And then we get to see how much junk food is served in our schools and that's there really no need for it, especially when we see one Wisconsin school for "troublesome" students that gets good results - and it just happens to serve its students healthy food.The scene with Subway spokesman Jared Fogle is very telling. When a young girl, trying to lose weight, observes that she can't afford to eat at Subway every day, you realize that it never dawns on the girl or her parents that she doesn't have to eat at Subway to be healthy, she should be eating healthily at home. But a Subway diet is what Jared's touting, after all.Although Spurlock admits it's unlikely anyone would turn to McDonald's for three squares per day, it's obvious there are many people who visit fast-food places several times a week, if not daily.The film provides enough caution to stop any sane person from eating fast food. Some of the nutritionists' observations throughout the film are particularly telling. The downside to all this is that obese people - and it's evident obesity is a national epidemic that's not being tackled properly, thanks in part to powerful lobbyists - then resort to drastic measures to lose weight. A flaw in Spurlock's film is when he shows a gastric bypass procedure (and you get to see it up close to the sounds of "The Blue Danube"), he fails to discuss the dangers involved with such surgery. And there are many.That McDonald's put an end to its super size offerings and the VP of the GMA lost his job - he acknowledges "we're part of the problem" - because of this film might be coincidental. Then again, maybe this film was the reason. "Super Size Me" entertains, informs and educates, all in a mostly humorous manner. The question is, will those who really need to see this film actually see it? I doubt it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A wake-up call</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>An independent film-maker in New York City eats at McDonald's three times a day every day for a month, just to see what happens to him. At the outset of this experiment, he is given a medical exam--and found to be in excellent health (at least physically, although his vegan girlfriend seems to question his mental state). This movie is not exactly scientific; it relies upon a "sample of one", the personal experience of an individual. But it's entertaining and insightful just the same. And a controlled scientific experiment, using thousands of test subjects doing the same thing, would probably yield similar results.In addition to his personal experience, he researches the fast food industry and its effects on society. As other reviewers point out, Eric Schlosser's book Fast Food Nation (Perennial, 2002) explores this subject in much more detail. Fast food and physical inactivity are directly linked to the growing problem of obesity.This is a cautionary tale for our times, a wake-up call. In the final analysis, your health depends on you--NOT Washington, Wall Street, Madison Avenue or Hollywood. If you don't believe me, just ask any vegan.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A smart, satirical documentary not to be missed.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SUPER SIZE ME is the story of a man (Morgan Spurlock) who decides to undergo an experiment in the name of science: For one month (30 days), he will eat nothing but McDonald's for breakfast, lunch, and dinner. If it's not sold over a McDonald's counter, he can't eat it. He must eat every item on the menu at least once... and see the results. We all know, to some extent, what the results will be, but they are shocking nonetheless. Meanwhile, as we watch Morgan undergo his experiment, we are shown a satirical and incredibly smart commentary on just how obese America is becoming and the possible reasons why.SUPER SIZE ME can easily be compared to another famous and popular documentary, BOWLING FOR COLUMBINE. Both deal with subject matters that should come as common sense to the majority of the population. School shootings and guns in America, bad. Eating lots and lots of fast food, bad. But both are able to open your eyes as to just how bad.The movie is so clever and discreet in the way it shows the definite link between America's obese population and the growing fast food industry... but, at the same time, it is not the fast food industry's fault alone. Morgan was a very healthy man before he began his experiment--and though the food had a great impact on him, he could have stopped the experiment if he wanted to, and he was advised to by his doctors. He chose to continue, showing that it's the individual's personal choice to eat at McDonald's (and other fast food restaurants), and just sit back and watch the results without doing anything about them. See what I mean? Very smart.SUPER SIZE ME is designed to make you think harder and look closer at what you think you already know. I think it succeeds. I even got into a debate with my brother on the way home about the lawsuits made against McDonald's and other fast food companies, and how right and legitimate they really are. I still think they're preposterous, but maybe not as much so as I once thought.SUPER SIZE ME is funny and pathetic, clever and sad. This film is one that successfully tip-toes around preaching, but instead shows you and educates you on just how harmful fast food is and can be, but that it's your decision to turn your life around. McDonald's can't do it for you. 8/10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Super Size Me Delivers</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Documentaries, no matter how engaging the theme, tend to run to fat ? almost any I have seen can be pruned a bit or the number of threads followed reduced. Exuberant authors of the genre (both non-fiction book and film writers) want to chase every issue in each and every parallel universe adjacent to the main theme. In Super Size Me we see a minimum of this problem (pursuing all issues) and a fairly focused topic with a minimal number of interruptions regarding related issues. We have a winner.The set-up to the premise that eating only fast food for thirty days might be unhealthy is both witty and informative. We see the author (Morgan Spurlock, 34 years old and relatively fit) subjected to a battery of medical tests to establish some baseline data and then watch him slowly decompose over the next month as he shovels in hamburgers and the like. I kept wondering how this would turn out, and there, as can be found in all good writing, is the hook.There is great usage of facts and interviews with knowledgeable people peppered throughout the narrative. We possibly could have done with a little less viewing of Spurlock eating; quicker glimpses of him horsing down food would have sufficed. But the overall effect, that of showing the types of food he was ingesting was probably served well enough in these vignettes.There are enough funny moments and the pace is overall darned good. This is a thought-provoking look at fast food restaurants ? something all Americans are at least familiar with if they are not regular diners at same. And there is a sensitive moment when Jared of Subway fame chats with an overweight girl and shares his own experience ? that he felt so different in his old body.Several times I was spurred to ponder my own eating and exercise regimens. I have recommended the film to a group of dietitians and nutrition experts, and I do the same for all. This movie is sure to invite discussion among many, and isn't that the point? A sure-fire Academy Award (Documentary) nominee, unlike most fast food restaurants, Super Size Me delivers.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Supersize Me is a 'Big Mac attack'</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Another shot-on-video winner at Sundance makes a big splash in commercial theaters. Is Morgan Spurlock's fast food expos "Supersize Me" really just a "Blair Sandwich Project"? Not quite. Unlike "Blair Witch," no one disappears in this movie. In fact, everyone gets noticeably bigger.I just left the Esquire Theatre in Clifton (Cincinnati) feeling sick to my stomach. I haven't walked out of a show this queasy since my last special effects double-feature at the Imax. Can't rightly say whether it was the graphic rectal exam and vomiting footage, or motion sickness induced by jarring hand-held videography. Calling this movie 'socially relevant' would be an understatement. That aside, "Supersize Me" is a must-see for advertisers, advertising professionals and TV commercial enthusiasts. Regardless of the shady motives of McDonald's and other crap-food corporations, Morgan Spurlock makes what may be the world's most compelling case for the power of television advertising.Animated sequences spell out the relationship between advertising investments and consumer buying/eating habits in a way that would seem too pedantic if Spurlock hadn't given due screen time to the impact of the supersize phenomenon on America's children.A few videotaped interviews did the trick for me. A family unsuccessfully tries to recite the Pledge of Allegiance, but when asked to list the ingredients in a Big Mac, one of the adults rattles off the "two all beef patties" mantra like a pro. In another interview, young kids hesitantly identify President George Washington's picture, and consistently draw blanks with a picture of Jesus Christ. Not surprisingly, every kid recognizes a drawing of Ronald McDonald with ease.The thesis of "Supersize Me" boils down to a soulless megacorporation whose marketing approach deliberately makes consumers addicted to unhealthy foods in unhealthy serving sizes - in much the same way tobacco companies earned trillions of dollars while effectively killing and disabling millions of Americans.The responsibility debate rages on, and I'll refrain from trying to settle it here (as if I could). "Supersize Me" deserves the attention it's getting, because whether we blame obese consumers or "fat food" companies like McDonald's, the number of American kids suffering from obesity has risen sharply in recent years.See it while it's still in theaters. Fast food chains reportedly spend $3 billion per year on advertising (bonus points for the reader who can tell me how much of that is for TV commercials), so don't expect "Supersize Me" to air as a "movie of the week" on any major television network in the foreseeable future.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>An art gallery is a dull place to a blind man</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I thought this movie was an excellent examination of the Obesity Epidemic in this country.That is what this movie is about. As Spurlock states more than once in the film, the big revelation isn't that eating McDonald's three meals a day for a month isn't good for him. The revelation is that 60% of U.S. adults are obese, the health costs for treating diabetes has doubled in five years, and 5-year-olds don't recognize Jesus, but they ALL know Ronald McDonald. This epidemic of obesity and diabetes is outrageous, and clearly we DON'T understand the ramifications of our pervasively bad diets, or this recent (in the last twenty years) problem wouldn't have come about.The cumulative argument of Spurlock's film is that our country is killing itself with bad eating and exercise habits, and if simply knowing that fast food is bad were enough, then we wouldn't all be a bunch of Fatties, now would we?Spurlock's film serves us a vital slap in the face. Many say that we know fast food is bad, and could choose not to eat it if we wanted. Spurlock himself espouses this confidence in free will early in the film.However, he builds a convincing case that the marketing machine that keeps us fat is far more powerful and insidious than any of us realizes. As the film explains, a child will be hit with 10,000 Ads a year for junk food, candy and soda - a powerful din that will be hard to outgrow as an "informed" adult.What is a parent's chances of cutting through this morass of advertizing? The film pointedly contrasts the billions of marketing dollars available to McDonald's and other junk food companies, with the fact that the Fitness Center he attends at the beginning of his experiment goes out of business before the month is up.His gimmick of eating McDonald's is a simple way to compress all the facets of the complex emotional relationship this country has developed with McDonald's and other junk food.The food tastes good, but he's often hungry soon after eating. McDonald's advertizes so heavily to children, that those same children grow in adulthood with a powerful association between McDonald's and the pure joy of childhood. The cocktail of fat, salt, sugar and caffeine that comprises a McDonald's meal is a potent formula to develop addiction in developing brains. The processes by which McDonald's creates products such as McNuggets are too horrific to show, so Spurlock resorts to clever (and amusing) animation to reveal how a creature can be machine processed into an engineered sustenance module.The fact that someone would post a review here saying that Spurlock is lying, and all the medical facts were made up by his doctor, is the most powerful evidence I can find that McDonald's marketing machine is working.That a rational adult would have test numbers 30 foot high on the silver screen in front of them, and STILL refuse to believe that this "food" is truly killing our country is a shocking reminder of how willfully blind we've grown to our own sorry state.Spurlock's movie, at different times hilarious and scary, is a powerful voice for self-examination and change. I hope its influence builds beyond McDonald's cancellation of the Super Size option.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The most disgusting movie of the year.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The most disgusting movie of the year is 'Super Size Me' because overindulging in fast food is inherently disgusting, and watching Morgan Spurlock do just that for 96 minutes is equally so. Spurlock spent a month eating McDonald's for three squares a day with unsurprising results.More than proving the danger to the liver and sex life, eating this much fast food shows once again that overdoing anything is usually detrimental to some part of a life. Spurlock had 3 physicians check him before he began, during the ordeal, and at the end (Do you get an idea of how dull the documentary can be at times?). Yet it has moments of humor, as when Spurlock cries out his pain in super-sized expletives.What good is it to endanger your liver and your lover? The corporation discontinued the super-sized menus after the release of the documentary; this is a good thing. What is not good, however, is that Spurlock is the center of all activity, and he is not sufficiently charismatic to carry it off. Spurlock seems almost quixotic as he faces the criticism of the doctors, who believe he may be irreparably damaging his liver, and his supportive girlfriend, who suffers from the impairment of his sexual appetite. Yet, when McDonald's announces discontinuance of its super- sizing promotion, his quest becomes a victory. The documentary also briefly touches on the argument about personal responsibility versus corporate guilt. Moreover, in his voice-over for the credits, Spurlock answers the most obvious criticism that his eating Mickey D' for every meal is egregious and compromising to objective analysis by remarking there are people who eat fast food regularly, some daily as he did. After a few photos of overweight Americans, his documentary suddenly is heroic, albeit still disgusting to watch. Because Spurlock's production company is called 'The Con,' and he could have gained 20 pounds overeating anything, I suspect he may have more in mind than helping his fellow Americans. As Brecht said, 'Grub first, then ethics.' Actually Spurlock has the ego but not the charm of Michael Moore ('Bowling for Columbine'), the showmanship but not the depth of Earl Morris ('Fog of War'). I would much rather have Moore on the screen even if he looks like someone who eats fast food more than once a month.This review is downsized in honor of all those Americans whose weight will fall and health rise because of 'Super Size Me.'</fullReview>

		<date>2004-05-11</date>
		<username>Greg (gregmoroberts@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A great documentary that educates without preaching</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Imagine eating only McDonalds food, three times a day for a full month. That is the premise of the new documentary written, directed and starring Morgan Spurlock. His intention? To monitor his health throughout the duration of the experiment to determine if eating massive amounts of junk food is really bad for the human condition.Sure it sounds suicidal, but when you stop to think about how many times you eat fast food throughout the week, maybe Morgan was not stretching the envelope too far here. In fact, he orates in a voiceover that 1 in every 4 people in America eat at a fast food offering every day, and as obesity continues to be a continuing problem with Americans (it will overtake smoking as the leading cause of health related problems), this film could not be more timely.Morgan first consults with three physicians and one nutritionist who takes readings, notes and blood tests from what can be considered ground zero. Then as the experiment goes into gear, regular check ups with the same doctors will track the progress.There is but three self imposed rules which he must abide. One, he could only eat was available over the counter. Two, he can only super size when asked and three, he had to eat everything on the menu at least once.Day one is portrayed as an eight year olds fantasy. Eating at McDonalds three times a day? Heaven. But by day three, things start to take a different turn. Morgan starts feeling ill while eating his quarter pounder super sized combo and ends up heaving outside his car 22 minutes after his meal began. This was the first of many moans I heard in the audience and I swear it was the last time I heard anyone reaching into their popcorn bag or sipping on their nearly completed large beverage.We then watch as Morgan goes through the 30-day ordeal and as his energy, sex life, emotions and physical strength are just as obvious as the 20 pounds he has packed on around his mid-section.Super Size Me premiered at the Sundance Film Festival where it was met with as much fanfare and soon with as much word of mouth as any documentary since Bowling For Columbine. Where as Bowling surrounded people and a situation that hopefully none of us ever have to deal with, Super Size Me hits home as we all indulge from time to time in snack foods or worry about how many notches we are not using in our belts. Morgan keeps the pace by if not entertaining us with his dry wit (he looks at a chicken McNugget and wonders what part of the chicken it might have come from), then we are aghast at the sight of someone getting his tummy-tucked or children able to identify a picture of Ronald McDonald over a picture of Jesus or the President of the United States.With both animated segments and humorous anecdotes (McGurrlges and McStomach Aches) intertwined with the reality that he put his health in extreme jeopardy to the point where doctors were telling him to forgo the project or risk permanent damage, Super Size Me delivers on almost every note. The trips to schools or to various cities and his observations while there are both poignant and respective. And his interviews with children or the cafeteria administrators at public schools were revealing without being exploitive. Unlike his most obvious comparative figure Michael Moore, Morgan was able to convey a thought without preaching. He does this in such a way as to not condemn McDonalds, but simply use their name and products to outline the obesity epidemic that has gripped the Western World.The film does seem to be about 10 minutes longer than it should be, but I would concede that I have always felt that documentaries should never be as long as your average comedy/adventure. However, if this be my only complaint, then I can learn to live with it.Super Size Me surprised me (say that five times) in its draw factor at the local theatre. A mid-afternoon showing on its third day of release brought out a packed house that laughed when they should have laughed and winced when they should have winced. And any director that can do that is ok in my books.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Fast Food Nation...What a Revelation!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Morgan Spurlock's documentary "Super Size Me" has a pretty simple premise: Spurlock himself will eat nothing but McDonald's for 30 days and see what happens. Going into the film, I had two thoughts: One, how can he spread this simple premise into a full 100 minute film? And two, I'm pretty sure I know the ending: he will get fat. DUH! Gee, what a revolutionary idea. And we're all supposed to applaud Mr. Spurlock for risking his health to teach us that eating only McDonald's will make you gain weight? I don't think so.That being said, the film did raise some good points. Mr. Spurlock fills up the hour and forty minutes with some great interviewing footage of everyone from cafeteria lunchladies to the former Surgeon General of the United States. He interviews people on the street to demonstrate America's general lack of knowledge about diet and health. He interviews kids who make poor choices at their lunchline in school to demonstrate that our children are learning poor eating habits early on (and who's to blame?). He interviews dieticians and doctors. He even interviews the lawyer in charge of prosecuting McDonald's on behalf of two overweight teenage girls who blame their obesity on the fast-food powerhouse. He tries, unsuccessfully, to interview someone from McDonald's. Overall, this footage was the best part of the film. Obesity is a growing problem in America that is in dire need of attention, and for that, I do applaud Mr. Spurlock.THAT being said, I think Mr. Spurlock confuses "documentary film-making" with "exhibitionism". AKA, I do not need to see him receiving a rectal exam, puking in the parking lot, eating up close, etc, to get the point of the film. Some people may be into this sort of thing, but it's just not my bag. It got pretty old just watching him eat and complain. This is not to say the entire subplot of his McDonald's binge was useless. It is interesting and quite frightening to see the acute change in his vital signs that occurred in a mere 30 days, and numbers do not lie. Ironically, his film reminded me of fellow documentarian Michael Moore, who might benefit from watching such a movie.Overall, I was not too impressed by the film - the editing, the cinematography, or the content - but I do think it's worth watching. I think it would have benefited from a different approach, but we'll forgive Mr. Spurlock since this was his first self-created work. But hey, what do I know? It seems the fast food companies have made an obvious effort to include healthier food on the menu, and perhaps this film is partly to thank. With all that being said, eat healthier, exercise, and enjoy the film.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>You want lies with that?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>To be honest, 'Super size me' is not my idea of a documentary. For some odd reason, I find documentaries, which the documentarian gets personally involved, is, well biased. Michael Moore gets away with it because, while he does have a personal agenda, and yes, his films are entertaining, he essentially lets the subjects speak for themselves. In the case of Super size Me, we have an individual, Morgan Spurlock, who makes a bet with himself that he will eat nothing but McDonald's food for a whole month and will record his progress through film. I'm sure you've read about the results of his experiment, so I won't go into detail about them here. But along the way, he visits various individuals across the country that tells him McDonald's related stories. Some are interesting, we meet the Subway guy, who ate nothing but Subway and lost weight, and we also meet a man who curiously eats nothing but Big Macs but hardly gains a pound. Why is this? We don't know, Spurlock doesn't really focus on this, which is a shame somewhat, because with every say 5 negative stories about Mickey D's, we get one somewhat curiously positive story about them. So Spurlock IS on a biased reporting binge. I mean, come on, going from complete vegetarianism to eating nothing but meat IS going to do something to you. It's called a SHOCK to your system. And nothing more. Don't get me wrong, I thought it was a really insightful film, but you gotta sometimes dig deeper when they're digging deep..</fullReview>

		<username>DARREN HORNE</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Entertaining, but nothing we didn't know already</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Food and sensible eating seems to be the topic of the day, with Jamie Oliver kicking at the door of number 10 to force the PM to notice that many of the current school dinners are literally destroying pupil's health. It's about time, we cheer, as the one thing that can unite the generations is a memory of school dinners that was far from appetising. Combine this with our quirky American cousins turning their litigious eyes to the fast food firms who they blame for making them fat, and you have a media feeding frenzy. Super Size Me is the journey of an American citizen, but this one isn't like the stereotypes, he has a girlfriend that is a vegan chef which, and as anyone guy that has ever dated a vegetarian will know this has a huge impact on your own diet. Spurlock is fit, healthy, attractive, and charismatic and appears intelligent, though his participation in this film may challenge that. With all the serious and intellectual media coverage the issue of healthy eating gets, Spurlock's approach is some what refreshing, if not ill-advised. To silence the McDonalds PR machine who claim their food will not make people fat, he decides to eat from their gourmet menu three times a day for one month, and reduce his exercise to the level of the average American. What begins as a fun and light hearted look at a guy gorging himself on Big Macs becomes a horrifying freak show of a man quickly and systematically destroying his body, causing weight gain of 20 pounds, mood swings, damage to his liver, and an increase in cholesterol. He feels fatigued and complains of chest pains, and perhaps most distressing of all is that his girlfriend starts to complain about his declining sexual ability!Spurlock's personal charm and amiability enhance the films appeal and carry us through the experiment, causing us to care for this brave, but inherently stupid yank. We feel for him as he undergoes the assault of burgers and fries, and when his girlfriend and doctors strongly advise he call off the whole thing, we yearn for him to pay heed. The one saving grace we have is the belief that his girlfriend will whip him back into shape, a feat that takes her a whole year to accomplish. It is easy to distance ourselves from these "silly Americans", after all, at times they seem as though they are another species entirely. But the resounding message that permeates this movie isn't a rant of the evil of the golden arches, but a reminder that the choice is ours, and we should choose to exercise and try no to eat more that one fast food meal a week. It is clear that Spurlock loves his food, and even after his near death experience it is likely he munches on the occasional quarter pounder. There is a fairly broad range of views present in this film, dietitians, nutritionists, lawyers and fast food fanatics. One overweight 14 yr old girl comments that a conference speaker at a Subway convention advised that eating two Subway meals a day instead of McDonald's will help her to lose weight. The girl's response is one of confusion as she can't afford two Subways a day, so how will she ever lose weight? On the other side of the argument there is the American that has eaten over 9, 000 Big Macs and remains very slim; though perhaps this is only an external illusion of health. However the real villains of this piece are the GIGANTIC sodas and evil fries, which should be avoided at all costs. What does all that soda do to their teeth? The acid must be having a party, no wonder the teeth whitening craze began in the USA!What does this film leave us with? Well, for what it is worth McDonalds stopped the super size option, but of course they claim, like our Labour government, that it had nothing to do with media pressure. Other than that we have a few laughs along with Spurlock, a highlight being the self aware comedic comparison of ham with heroin. We share his shock at McDonalds aggressive child orientated marketing campaign, the contents of a chicken McNugget, and the revelation that the definition of what a calorie is eludes us. As a gonzo documentary about fast food it is superb viewing, but like most films of this type it is only worth watching once.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-10-15</date>
		<username>gtmoz</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Entertaining, even if manipulative.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A very (emphasis on very) entertaining piece of anti-giant corporation propaganda. Director Morgan Spurlock admits in this documentary the vast majority of people are aware of the fact fast food is not good for you, or anyone. Yet he subjects himself to an unscientific study of the short-term effects of an all-fast-food diet, to promote his own agenda which, in my opinion, panders more to an avoidance of claiming responsibility for one's own actions and pushing the blame onto the big money. The real question to be asked (one which will never be answered) is whether big money is really an evil entity. While McDonalds, and all fast food restaurants, do offer food which is high in fat, cholesterol, salt and other ingredients which can and will be detrimental to a person's health, they also provide jobs (not the best, of course) for certain people who would not be able to obtain a job anywhere else. They also (probably for tax write-offs) donate millions of dollars to various charities and, in the case of McDonald's, founded Camp Ronald McDonald.One of the aspects of the McDonald's corporation Spurlock attacks is advertising. Replete with examples and statistics, the film presents a hefty argument which says the average child sees approximately 10,000 adverts per year, and, you guessed it: many of these are selling toys sold with fast food products. Okay, so the fast-food corporations do pander to children as much as alcohol and Yoplait ads pander to college students and trend-following young mothers (respectively). The children may not be directly responsible for being easily sold on advertisements; they are not quite old enough to understand marketing ploys. However, the parents ARE responsible for the proper feeding and nutrition of their children and should, therefore, exercise some discretion and control instead of caving to a whining kid's tantrums. However, as much as everybody complains about no-one taking responsibility for their own actions, there is still nothing apparently being done about frivolous lawsuits blaming people who should, by all means, have no claims of responsibility for some drunk college student taking a plunge out a window. Off the subject (yet still related) recent legislation has members of the democratic party in government overruling new laws directed at sporting events. In essence, what was proposed was that participants of a sport would not have the provisions to sue if they were injured while playing the game. These non-accountable injuries would have been limited to those incurred while playing the game to the letter of the law. Personally, that makes perfect sense. There are risks to playing sports, especially when there is a lot of roughhousing involved (such as wrestling, football, golf). But to some. the ability to sue a school district or league thereby draining funds from personal levels (families who hire attorneys, time spent away from a job, etc.) and governmental (school districts, time spent in court, etc.) is a right. Now this may seem hypocritical, but it is a right, and it is a right which should not be taken away. But it still comes down to one thing: people should take responsibility for their own actions and in the case of children, someone needs to claim responsibility for them.Back to the scientific nature of Spurlock's adventure. His experiment was lacking in a control group. The purpose of a control group is to help validate his (or any scientific) claims that the high-fat, high-carb, high-sugar diet would be as harmful to everyone as it was to him. Of course, it probably would be although there is a man in the documentary who eats multiple Big Macs a day and still maintains a healthy-looking physique and an admirable cholesterol level of approximately 140. He also does not follow correct procedure when reporting to have blood drawn to be used in blood tests. In order to get clean results from bloodwork, doctors advise a fasting period anywhere from 12 hours and up before the draw to ensure unbiased results. While the film does not specify if Spurlock actually followed correct procedure, it also does not say whether he did. Therefore, I am of the opinion he did not. More likely, he stuffed his face with some grease-laden concoction hours, or possibly minutes, before reporting to the doctor's exam.I am not denying or attempting to soften the effects a fast food diet would have on someone, but there are other factors at work in his 'experiment.' First of all, Spurlock is a New Yorker and does not own a car. By his own admission he would, before indulging in McDonald's, walk mile after mile per day. At the onset of his diet, he equipped himself with a pedometer to limit his walking to one mile a day or less. What else? He doubled his daily caloric intake, sometimes topping 5,000 calories per day. By reducing exercise to a bare minimum and upping the amount of calories consumed two-fold, virtually anyone would undergo the same physical changes for the worse.By and large, the food he consumed was and still is, obviously, less-than-healthy. However, consuming to Spurlock's excessive level and minimizing physical activity, the effects are obvious and no-one needs a documentary to tell them that.He succeeded in presenting McDonald's as an evil, faceless corporate giant, especially by the cleverly -edited sequence near the end of the picture where he is attempting to contact a McDonald's corporate representative to schedule an interview...an interview which, surprise!, never happens. The audience, however, is left in the dark as to why this interview never occurs. On a side note, there are many vague instances in this movie. Vague because the audience doesn't have the same points of reference Spurlock does. For example, when he is visiting with the doctors about the results of his bloodwork, the cutting and editing is presented in such a way that what he is doing seems incredibly dangerous, as though he has a death wish. Then, when the doctor is discussing the results, he only seems to talk about cholesterol and make references that Spurlock has the liver of a very sick man. Furthermore, unless viewers are educated or doctors themselves, making sense of the numbers is quite a task, a task Spurlock takes no time defining.As a whole, the film was very entertaining. There was enough humor and plenty of pop references to keep any hip and cool college student satisfied. There was drama and suspense (Will he go through with it? Will he survive?) Spurlock even managed to paint himself as a likable character who, for the duration of the film, doesn't seem like such a spin doctor or manipulator. Will I ever eat fast food again? Of course. Sometimes it just sounds good.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>I still love fast food!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have loved fast food since about 1968, when McDonald's introduced their Big Mac (for 25 cents!) and my mother took me and my brother there to sample this new hamburger: "two all-beef patties, special sauce ... etc." I love the fact that you can buy a meal which is cheap, fast, tasty -- and you don't have to pay a tip! -- even though nutrition was never part of the equation. Obviously, these reasons are attractive to many other Americans, and increasingly, customers throughout the world. I even considered creating a coffee-table book on the origins of individual fast food restaurants. I have endured criticism from friends for my fast food ways. So of course I rented Super Size Me. I have already cut back on my fast food consumption in the interest of reducing my fat and calorie intake, and Super Size Me definitely inspired me to eat even less. Morgan Spurlock's relaxed yet funny personality is a nice contrast to Michael Moore. Of course eating at McDonald's three times a day is going to affect your health! However, I wasn't prepared for the extent that Spurlock's mental as well as physical health was affected. I would have been more interested in seeing how much one McDonald's meal per day would affect someone's health, because this is a more realistic level of consumption.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-10-11</date>
		<username>jkaden</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>I Want Me, and Lots of It</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's like "Roger and Me", but about fast food. A guy eats only McDonald's for 30 days, and we go along for the ride. He starts out at optimal health, and by the end he's gained 20 pounds and has a list of ailments. Each of the three doctors he has monitoring this experiment is surprised by the drastic deterioration of his health. Junk food is bad for you; even worse for you than people think. This much is clear.What's unclear is why Morgan Spurlock is doing this to himself. Perhaps a more salient question is: Why is Mr. Spurlock filming himself doing this? And we have our answer. Mr. Spurlock has to film himself doing something, and fast food is the vehicle.He is Mr. Perfect. His health is perfect, as verified (on camera) by the various health experts he consults before Mickey-D-Day. He has a cute girlfriend, who is a vegan chef. Oh, but Mr. Spurlock isn't a vegan; no no, that would be too weird for Mr. Perfect. He's not even a vegetarian. Just a good old, red-blooded American carnivore.He doesn't drink ("not now"), doesn't smoke ("I used to, but I stopped"), and doesn't do drugs ("not for a long time"). From this we can infer a party-hearty youth, now tempered by maturity, a la Henry V. He has an active sex life, just in case we missed the girlfriend earlier, or thought maybe they were just friends or something. So, good: not gay, or at least not flaming, and no serious erectile dysfunction. This last one's important, though, because, just in case we missed it the first two times, the girlfriend underscores it later for us in the movie.In other words, he's Mr. Perfect. This movie also gives us plenty of caught-on-tape evidence that he's funny, smart, and cool. "Super Size Me" as a title is actually quite revealing here: "I Want Me, and Lots of It" is a fair description of the movie's content.There are a couple of scenes that are discordant, like when he shows mugshots of Jesus and Ronald McDonald to the kids, and has people try to say the Pledge of Allegiance and then the Big Mac song. I can see that he puts that stuff in for demographics; he had to appeal to the heartland. Your random NYC resident, for example, probably isn't going to blanch and grow faint at the thought that "Your average kindergartener knows Ronald McDonald's face, but not Jesus'." If someone told me that, and it was a perfect universe, I'd say, "Well, it's progress from when I was young; now, when we get so the kids don't recognize either of 'em, then we'll have something."So, anyhow, he's obviously giving some thought to the audience. But this extends to his depictions of actual events. Mr. Spurlock is so self-conscious that even clearly spontaneous events seem almost scripted. He keeps on darting glances at the camera, as if to check on my reaction. "Am I still Mr. Perfect?" his eyes ask me.Of course, he has to be perfect, or this movie wouldn't work. "Ahhh, that dumb kid was headed for liver failure anyhow; he smoked weed and was fat to start with!" So he, no doubt, cleaned up his act and whipped himself into shape before filming. Now there's a story for you! Mr. Pot-Head Schlub has 30 days to become Mr. Perfect, to prepare for a 30-day junk food binge. If this part were in the movie, I'd be writing a more positive review.In my particularly uncharitable moments, I imagine that he sees himself as Gandhi, starving himself to stop a civil war, or Jesus, being crucified to absolve us of our sins. To save the world from poison, he poisons himself. And here's our tie-in to "Life of David Gale", which I now see has been on my mind because it glorifies the same kind of martyrdom, and exhibits the same symptoms of phoniness.And yet, the truth is the truth, even when poured from such a flawed vessel as Mr. Spurlock. Fast food is a terrible industry, and people need to know that. Furthermore, this movie has apparently forced McDonald's to discontinue the Super Size program. So we have a movie actually doing some good in the world, and this I applaud. I applaud Mr. Spurlock for his work, and for his stand against the fast food industry.Still, as a movie, as a work of art, I have to say I strongly dislike this movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I decided that it was prudent to have my Big Mac, Medium Fries, no drink, before catching this movie. Nevertheless upon entering the cinema I was feeling slightly nauseous. Perhaps called EATING IN OR OUT, or maybe perhaps EXPLOITING THIRD WORLD WORKERS AND FIRST WORLD CONSUMERS. Anyway I am drifting, the subject of this 'Michael Moorish' style documentary is the obesity epidemic sweeping America caused mainly by the junk food industry. Thus our hero and documentary maker, 'Morgan Spurlock' starts out as a super fit health food fanatic. However he decides to swap all that, by living off nothing but the products of McDonalds menu for a complete month. Meanwhile four top medical and nutritional specialists monitor his body to see the effect that three junk food meals a day will have on his general health. The three golden rules of this experiment are: (1) He can only eat/drink what is on the McDonalds menu. (2) He has to have every item on the menu at least once during the month. (3) If the person serving asks him whether he wants to super size his meal (go large in UK parlance), he has to go along with it.Now I have to go state my general position here, namely criticising McDonalds is to me like saying bad things about Christmas. However 'Spurlock' is in effect using McDonalds merely as an example to criticise the whole US fast food, fizzy drink, saturated fat, billion dollar industry. He also acknowledges that no one is going to eat three McDonalds a day (that is not to say however that people aren't using it once a day). However for McDonalds or fast junk food, read chocolate, alcohol, smoking, prescription drugs etc. etc. Thus these things are made to be addictive, so that we keep buying them, the economy keeps on turning, health care professionals are kept in employment, and the world goes round. So yes we should all eat better, but not everyone has the time, money or access to prepare/eat healthy food three times a day. Besides these things taste so darned good.That said this is a highly entertaining documentary, and every bit as good as if it had been made by 'Michael Moore' and not 'Morgan Spurlock'. In fact giving that 'Moore' is a couple of pounds overweight anyway, 'Spurlock' is much better suited to such a quest. Needless to say that by the end of the month, 'Spurlock' is 25 pounds heavier, and the numbers on his medical charts are flipping off the scale. All this much to the chagrin of his vegetarian fascist girlfriend, whose remarks about his deteriorating sexual performance are just so classy.Anyway one of my two favourite moments are when after been given his first hit of a super size meal from a McDonalds drive in, twenty two minutes later 'Spurlock' has to puke it all up out of the car window. The second is following an explanation by a psychologist that McDonalds creates a pleasurable imprint on children from a very young age, 'Spurlock states that when he has a kid, every time he drives past McDonalds he is going to punch it in the face.Yet this documentary is not just about entertainment, it is informative too. For instance there is actually no sugar in McDonalds French Fries, but their Salad has as much sugar as a Big Mac. That is why you see, I always have fries with my Big Mac, and never go near the Salads.Mike filmfan99uk</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Sad but true</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a very straightforward, in your face documentary about the effects that fast food (focused mainly on McDonald's) has on people. Independent filmmaker Morgan Spurlock uses himself as a guinea pig to test what effects eating nothing but McDonald's for a month would have on a person. He has to eat only what is sold at McDonald's, including water. If McDonald's doesn't sell it, he can't eat it. He has to eat three square meals a day. At first, he can't even hold the food down.The results are rather appalling. He gains 25 lbs. in just one month. His liver functions start dwindling, he has no sex drive, he is always bloated, and he literally gets addicted to the stuff! But this is just part of the documentary. Throughout the whole movie there are interviews with people off the street for various things, interviews with important people in the food industry, a couple of schools are visited to see how much (or little) physical education children get, plus much more. The whole movie is filled with interesting facts from beginning to end. Even the question "where does consumer responsibility end and corporate responsibility begin" is discussed, along with the psychology involved in advertising fast food to children. Enough said about this movie, go see it for an educational and enlightening experience. And if you are overweight, hell, it might even be inspirational for you.</fullReview>

The human body gets conditioned to a pattern of foods, and for most people, these results would be totally unrealistic. I do not LOVE the big chain. Eating lots of food and not doing exercise isn't the best way to go about things? No, it isn't. Most of these we've all seen the likes of many times before, and although the none-too-subtle message is that fast food is bad for us, all this movie did was leave me with a bit of a craving to go straight to McDonalds and pig out.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>"There is a big, nappy hair in my sundae."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Super Size Me is the hugely successful documentary that follows Morgan Spurlock, a healthy 32 year-old filmmaker, as he goes on a month long diet consisting of nothing but food from McDonald's. If McDonald's doesn't sell it, he's not eating it. He's also set a goal of limiting his exercise to 5,000 steps per day. According to the movie this is the average amount walked by most Americans. The results: a 24.5 pound weight gain, increased cholesterol, liver damage, and, if you believe Spurlock and his girlfriend, depression and a decreased sex drive. If you believe the results the film presents, it's quite eye-opening.After having watched Super Size Me for the first time, my initial reaction is that while it's a reasonably entertaining way to spend an hour and a half, the logic and methodology are so flawed that it's difficult for me to take it too seriously. To begin with, who eats at McDonald's three meals a day? And why go for the Double Quarter Pounder with Cheese for your first meal? And do you really need to add a shake to every order? I'm not defending McDonald's at all ? their food is some of the most unhealthy junk you can find. But if you're going to go into an experiment and eat like Spurlock does, the results are predictable. The most shocking thing to me about the outcome is how shocked his doctors appear. Spurlock set out to prove something and did everything in his power to make sure he got the outcome he desired. Otherwise his movie and efforts would have been a failure. In the end, I don't care what diet you're on or where you get your food, if you consume an average of 5,000 calories a day and limit your exercise, you're going to gain weight and do damage to your body. Is that really surprising? As for Spurlock's claims of depression and a reduced sex drive, they're just that ? claims. There's no empirical data to support these claims. They sound good, but can't be scientifically documented. Again, I'm no supporter of McDonald's, but if you're going to bash the company, you're going to need a little more "evidence' than this.Even though I have problems with Super Size Me, it has made me re-think some of my own food choices ? and not just for myself, but for my child as well. We still eat at McDonald's, but try to make smarter choices. So I suppose that on some level I have to admit that the movie worked on me. I guess a rating of 5/10 would be about right.</fullReview>

For those who don't know SSM is the best political documentary this year- far better than MM's lamentable Fahrenheit 9/11.Both films have baddies- W. Bush &amp; McDonald's, both have their filmmakers as stars- MM &amp; MS, but MS has a few things going for him that MM doesn't- 1) a penchant for being honest, 2) a message that affects all his viewers, &amp; 3) a sense of self-deprecation. He demonstrates this by deciding to guinea pig his health in testing whether or not McDonald's fast food is really bad for you. He vows to eat nothing but McDonald's for 1 month straight.He starts off with a routine physical exam- which shows him to be in above average physical shape for a man I'd reckon is in his early 30s. His month-long odyssey will take him across the country, &amp; to the brink of serious health issues. This is all documented by a cardiologist, gastroenterologist, internist, nutritionist/dietician, &amp; physical trainer- not to mention his vegan chef girlfriend, Alexandra Jamieson, whose almost self-parodically the typical bored white girl with no better cause to be for?. All in all Super Size Me is a terrific film that entertains &amp; enlightens. Can someone eat healthily at a McDonald's? Not really- but the point has gotten to the point that that's not really the point. The point is this can kill you as slowly &amp; surely as cigarettes, alcohol, &amp; drug abuse. Here's hoping that MS becomes as big a name as MM, so that he will be able to tackle a varied subject palette. While MM is all windy style over substance MS shows that substance has its adherents too- you just have to remember to pass the gas.</fullReview>

Do American eat too much fast food? Sure. But I doubt even the heaviest consumers think it's good for you. I do think this might be a good movie to show teenagers, who might actually not understand the realities behind the fast food industry.Spurlock is an engaging presence. He manages to entertainingly dramatize what could have been a lot of boring information. I think the stunt's silly,but I have to hand it to Spurlock -- it definitely took guts. As the amazed reactions of his doctors show, later in the movie.The stunt is also important in film terms, as it neatly avoids the one big trap these kind of movies tend to have: a sense of elitism, moral condemnation, a tut-tutting at the masses who behave badly and the like. There is some of that here -- mostly embodied by Spurlock's truly annoying girlfriend -- but Spurlock, for the most part, avoids that. After all, he's on the same diet, right? He's part of them; he's not really talking down to them.I did think Spurlock glided over some elements in his argument that would've been worth pursuing -- especially the notion from the lawyer that they were suing McDonald's mainly for the cash. I also think the movie is terribly TV bound: it feels like a particularly good PBS documentary, not a movie. I'm glad I didn't see it on the big screen.The DVD has some good bonus features: SUPER SIZE ME in fact is a good example of a movie you should rent, not see in the theater.</fullReview>

		<username>RACapowski</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>And Michael Moore is annoying?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Super Size Me" is the type of movie that's better to catch in 15-second snippets on news programs than actually view at full length. It conducts a worthwhile experiment, if one with a dunderheadedly obvious conclusion, but conducts it in such an obnoxious manner than it's better to skip straight to the conclusion. One is surprised to see such a film done at all, but it is not done well.The central problem: Spurlock is unlikable, and he finds himself an limitlessly fascinating subject. Endlessly he hams for the camera, and he's all too happy to chronicle every smirk, chortle, twitch, and McJoke. *Especially* the McJokes - if a "McCoronary" or "McComa" is funny once, it's funny *every* time, darn it. I'd had it with him fifteen minutes in. The movie's like watching someone's home movies for ninety minutes - those of someone who constantly elbows you in the ribs, prodding "c'mon, c'mon, ain't this GREAT?", and insists on narrating every banality."Super Size Me" would not seem to need padding, yet it repeats itself endlessly. There's about ten minutes of doctors praising Spurlock for his perfect health, trim figure, basement-level cholesterol, and on and on, until we suspect the doc has gone beyond notation into braggadocio. The supersize portions at McDonald's are astounding, yes, but the movie takes way too much time to note this for each item, particularly when loudly driven home by Spurlock's faux, stagey marvel.Spurlock has a frat-boy sensibility. Grotesque caricatures serve as interstitials. The camera will focus pseudo-casually on the pin-ups in Spurlock's apartment to sneak some breasts into the picture. He swears like a 12-year-old - just to demonstrate he can. When he pukes, we have box seats. He chats as happily about his genital problems as any nightmare customer in line at the pharmacy.The in-depth, context-lending coverage of the obesity health issue is just a series of talking heads; the sleaziest 12-minute "Dateline" spot has more depth. There is a horror to Spurlock eating himself to near-death; this is as vivid and grotesquely memorable an illustration of a statistical reality as ever committed to film or memory. Spurlock's commitment to his cause cannot be impugned. "Super Size Me", though, relies on an emotional appeal, and as such, it's a particularly tiring one - a 90-minute "No, you fool! Don't go into the basement alone!" horror-movie moment, the indie version of those fundamentalist haunted houses that feature mock-ups of abortions and the puncture-filled torments of the Karo-decked damned - venture not here, children, lest ye share this gruesome fate! The film's self-fascination and irritating affectations are as memorable as the experiment it chronicles. Wake me when the book comes.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2005-09-23</date>
		<username>Merwyn Grote (majikstl@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Stupefy Me</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In this pseudo-documentary, filmmaker Morgan Spurlock wants to prove that McDonald's restaurants (and all other fast food restaurants for that matter) are evil corporations endangering America by serving up a defective product. To prove his questionable assertion, he ignored common sense and ate nothing but McDonald's food three times a day for one month, making an effort to overeat, even to the point of throwing up. Ignoring the obvious, that eating too much of anything and basing your diet on a limited array of foods will be bad for your health, he staged a scientifically unsound experiment designed to reach a foregone conclusion. In effect, he tried to reveal truth by being shamelessly dishonest.It is appropriate that Spurlock takes aim at the fast food industry with his first crusading (and condescending) documentary, because he has produced a film that is the fast food equivalent of a documentary. Lockstepping in line with the success of films by hacks like Michael Moore, the ambitious Spurlock -- unsatisfied with the boring talking-head style lecturing that makes up most of his film -- resorts to junk food journalism by staging the aforementioned cheesy publicity stunt. And, as is the wont of modern journalism, he has made the film more about him than about his topic.Despite his obvious bias against the restaurant chain, Spurlock seems to have no qualms about emulating the very things about McDonalds that he calms to find so insidious. Admittedly, he has a relevant message he wants to serve up, but he has a problem: the repetitive cant served up by the talking heads of Doctor This and Professor That, while perfectly healthy, is also about as tasty and appetizing as boiled spinach and puree 'd lima beans. So, like Mickey D, Spurlock wants to offer a more trendy, more consumer-friendly menu, in this case, anti-corporation paranoia with a side order of grandstanding egomania, washed down with a 64 oz. cup of self-righteousness. To this end, he resorts to a high pressure ad campaign (his all-I-can-eat publicity stunt), toy surprises (cartoons, cutesy graphics and chirpy pop songs) and even a spokesclown (Mr. Spurlock himself), all to convince us to swallow his dubious and honesty-deficient propaganda.From a business point of view, McDonald's is a great American success story. The chain satisfies its millions of customers with a menu that most people think tastes pretty good, that is efficiently prepared, generously portioned and relatively inexpensive, all in an atmosphere that makes a mundane event enjoyable. Its food is not and never has been intended to be a primary food source or the mainstay of a healthy diet. Spurlock and his chosen experts attack the McD's product line for not being something it was never intended or pretended to be. One could rightly expect Spurlock to hit himself over the head with a Black &amp; Decker hammer to prove their inadequacy as fly swatters or to drive a Chevrolet off a cliff to point out how ineffective they are as airplanes.I have little doubt that most if not all of the research data in SUPER SIZE ME is factual. But being truthful is not necessarily the same thing as being honest. It is not the accusations made by Spurlock's droning array of talking heads that are dishonest, but his ultimate conclusion. Logic dictates that if a person overeats, barring genetic quirks, the responsibility for being overweight goes to the overeater. But not in our "nation of victims" society. The fast food industry is the villain for -- gasp! -- selling products that consumers want and enjoy. The fiends! Even as his reckless behavior illustrates the dangers of not taking responsibility for one's actions, Spurlock continues to place the blame on McDonalds. Why? Because he realizes that nothing is as unappetizing to an audience than a finger-wagging sermon; so he coats his McMessage with an "It's not your fault" honey glaze. McDonald's is making you fat, he suggests, and an individual's act of mouth-stuffing over-indulgence is totally irrelevant. This makes his Unhappy Meal a bit easier to sell and to swallow.Also, Spurlock gets to play David to the fast food Goliath. By casting himself as a martyr, nobly sacrificing his health and possibly his life for the good of the hamburger eating public, Spurlock gets to massage his ego and absolve others of responsibility. He is crucifying (or Frenchifrying) himself for the betterment of mankind, or so he would have us believe. But if a few weeks of bad eating and the gaining of 20 pounds is actually endangering his life, then Spurlock has no right to claim he started out in perfect health. Indeed, too many moments in Spurlock's McMelodrama seem staged and performed; his angst-ridden, soul-searching stares as he contemplates his impending fast food doom and his belly aching about his belly aches seem as fishily pre-processed as a filet-o-fish special.I suppose that Spurlock had to resort to some sort of grandstanding, because otherwise he doesn't offer much to sink one's teeth into. Little of what he has to say seems freshly cooked and much of it has been setting under the heat lamp for quite a long time, leftovers from the myriad news reports and TV specials that have come before. He thinks his personal Big Mac attack provides hardy food for thought, but to steal a competitor's famed line: "Where's the beef?"</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Nothing That Happens Rings True</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>That eating every day at McDonald's (or any other fast food restaurant) is unhealthy goes without saying. That doing so can have very bad consequences for our health in the long run is equally obvious. And I don't object to having these facts pointed out. And yet... for a supposed documentary, "Supersize This" was remarkably implausible. I found myself doubting the truth of almost everything I saw.I don't doubt for a second that Spurlock gained weight during his experiment. But I don't buy for a second that eating at McDonald's made him vomit. Nor do I buy the idea that he'd suffer from impotence or develop a life-threatening liver condition after just a few weeks. In short, I think Spurlock and his doctors are liars.That's not to say their overall points aren't valid. Again, eating junk food on a regular basis DOES have bad long-term consequences. But a month is not the long term! It certainly isn't long enough for any serious health effects to show themselves. And that, I think, is why Spurlock felt the need to exaggerate and distort the truth. Mind you, I think Spurlock is a decent person, and I'm sure he is lying with the best and most noble of intentions, just as many anti-smoking crusaders lie about the effects of smoking second-hand smoke. They're so sure that they're right, and they're so sure of the righteousness of their cause, they can't see how ANY tactic could be wrong.If Spurlock were here with me, I suspect he'd tell me, confidentially, "Okay, okay, so I exaggerated a bit. So I staged the vomit scene. So I got the doctor to overstate the damage to my liver. I admit, I'm a little bit of a fraud. But where's the harm? If I manage to scare some people into eating less junk, that's a GOOD thing, isn't it?"Well, no. Lying isn't a good thing, even if the desired end result is a noble one. THat's why, even though Spurlock is a likable, charming and even funny guy, I can't recommend this movie. At all.</fullReview>

		<username>Prof_Lostiswitz</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>Greasy Kid Stuff</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The art of the documentary is really going downhill. In the 70s and 80s people perfected a way of making documentaries without any narrative, just letting the sequence of images tell the story. Koyaanisqatsi, Atomic Caf, Gimme Shelter are examples of this; they are all works of art. Making and understanding such a doc requires a greater amount of effort, but it yields a more satisfying experience.Nowadays filmmakers have reverted to the primitive form of beating us over the head with their message, telling us at every turn what we should think. Supersize Me falls exactly into this category, we are given no chance to think for ourselves. The message is a good one, no quarrel there, but ultimately the film resembles the fast food it criticizes - superficially appealing but unedifying. There has been a whole slew of docs like this - Bowling for Columbine, Fahrenheit 9/11, The Corporation - and I have been unwilling to take issue because I agree with the messages they contain. But I don't want this unimaginative and conventional style to become the norm! I ask documentary-makers to study Godfrey Reggio and the Maysles brothers, so they can learn how to purvey their message with elegance.</fullReview>
	</review>
