		<shortDescription>Spoiler: He Gets Fat!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The number of entertainment films masquerading as documentaries has increased exponentially in recent years, spurred, I'm sure, by the somewhat curious success of Michael Moore. Gone are the days when filmmakers and their associates would dig through musty stacks of records or film archives to assemble a comprehensive tome on a given subject. Now, it appears, all one needs is a camera and a bullhorn.Which is sad, because the stated premise of this film is intriguing; if a person were to eat nothing but food available from a fast food chain, what would be the effect on that person's health? We may find the answer to that question someday, but you won't find it in this movie. No, what you'll find is a man who sets out to defame an industry and willfully risks his own health to do so. In the interest of "science", Mr. Spurlock starts eating everything on the McDonald's menu, choosing not on the basis of his appetite but in an attempt to eat everything on the menu. Always "supersize"ing his order whenever the choice was offered, finishing everything he ordered regardless of his appetite while simultaneously halting his routine exercise regimen, he gained weight and lost muscle tone! Amazing! And entertaining, in a "Fear Factor" sort of way. But a documentary? Only if you consider it as such in the context of documenting his attempt to defame McDonald's. Sadly, I'm sure that this won't be the last we'll hear from Mr. Spurlock. In these days of television shows like "Trading Spouses" and "My Big Fat Obnoxious Fianc" his work just may be what the public wants. But, to me, it was 96 minutes of my life that I'll never get back...</fullReview>

		<date>2010-05-17</date>
		<username>Andrew Phillips</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>So who's to blame again?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The 2004 documentary "Super Size Me" takes on one of the most universally recognized icons of the modern world: the fast-food giant, McDonalds. The 100-minute film follows director, actor and lab rat, Morgan Spurlock as he follows a regimen of nothing but McDonalds fare for breakfast, lunch and dinner, every day for 30 days. During this time he is closely monitored by three doctors, a dietician, a physical therapist, his girlfriend, and (of course) a full compliment of cameras.The changes that occurred in Morgan's body and psyche were quite dramatic. At the beginning of the experiment, his health was attested to be above-average by his three doctors. At the end of his month-long McDiet, Morgan had gained twenty-four and a half pounds. His cholesterol shot up 65 points and his body fat percentage went from eleven to eighteen percent. His doctors told him he had nearly doubled his risk of coronary heart disease, and had made himself twice as likely to have heart failure. In addition to the damage done to his body, Morgan said, "I felt depressed and exhausted most of the time, my mood swung on a dime and my sex life was non-existent. I craved this food more and more when I ate it, and got massive headaches when I didn't." (Spurlock) The journey ends with a memorable rant where the fast food industry is portrayed as a powerful machine that is continually moving forward. It will always act in its own interests, heedless of the cost to the general population. "I guess the real question is," Spurlock asks after showing a photo of a cemetery, "who do you want to see go first? You? Or them?" "Super Size Me" does an excellent job of showing how fast food is a (ahem) large problem in America, and that the dangers of eating fast food are (pardon the pun) grave. However, the film failed to so much as mention the root cause of this problem, or provide a viable solution.Is the solution to sue the super-companies who own the fast food restaurants for making their customers fat? Is it to institute a "fat tax" to make it less affordable for big fat Joe to stuff his big fat face with big fat Mac? Or is the solution to spread mud in the face of the fast food industry until the public is no longer appetized by what they see? There are those who believe that these are real solutions to our nation's obesity woes.However, as the film itself states, companies are going to sell what the public demands. In a capitalistic world, what people want is what they get. Turns out America wants fast food, and lots of it, and amazingly enough, there are people out there who are willing to sell it to them. What's more, there are other people who will try to fix their bloated bodies when they break down, and still other people who will sell them over-sized coffins when they die (that would be me).However, has anybody asked why people want fast food? Could it possibly be because nobody is cooking at home any more? The problem is not that there is too much fast food out there, or that it is too available, too inexpensive, too advertised, or even too fattening. The problem is that there is no real food inside the home, because there is nobody there to cook it.While it is nice to wax eloquent on the evils of big business, the only way that change is going to amount to anything is if it begins at the individual level. When individual men and women make wise choices, they lead healthy lives and build healthy families. When healthy families provide their children with three healthy meals a day, they will raise a generation who will want to do the same. When generations of people are getting their meals at home every day, in a healthy, enjoyable environment, McDonalds will go out of business.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>LillaMy</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Stumbling over the very foundation stones of film making</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Let's start with the bad points. Super Size Me was made on a semi-amateur basis. But even only a semi-professional documentary cannot commit the grave mistakes of basic film making rules. Good footage has 4 characteristics: the images must be sharp, properly exposed, stable and cropped. Footage out of focus is unacceptable even for home made family videos, let alone a documentary to be distributed. The unsteady images can be justified, and at some points unavoidable, but it usually just gives the spectator a headache. Also lighting - even in a documentary - should be taken care of. Many images were overexposed. The crop defines what is important, and it is presented in a balanced layout. This was many times completely missing. Film editing programs are now easy at hand and have lots of lovely built-in gadgets, that are fun to play with, but give the film a very cheap appearance. The overload of these gadgets makes Super Size Me really messy. For the content, the idea is interesting. But in the end, I knew no more of the subject than what is general knowledge. The only thing that shocked me, was that someone can mistake Jesus for George Bush, no matter how young they are. The actual outcome is shallow. I was left with the image that no adult knows what a calorie is, which I hope is not the real case. The storyline is hard to follow, because Morgan Spurlock has dragged in so many different subjects: the obesity of the nation, the effect of advertising, McDonald's food, general knowledge of Americans, the lawsuit against McDonald's, eating habits of schools... Each topic vast enough to make a documentary in itself. I myself found some of the scenes disgusting and very much out of point. I cannot see why a documentary on McDo's food should tell about fat surgery, which again is a topic in itself. Puking scenes brings to mind a teenage party with excessive drinking and its results. To the good points. It was funny. At times. Morgan Spurlock has a positive air around him. I hope also the film has some impact on the food industry, and especially school nutrition. All in all, a long home made film worth seeing once.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Nothing That Happens Rings True</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>That eating every day at McDonald's (or any other fast food restaurant) is unhealthy goes without saying. That doing so can have very bad consequences for our health in the long run is equally obvious. And I don't object to having these facts pointed out. And yet... for a supposed documentary, "Supersize Me" was remarkably implausible. I found myself doubting the truth of almost everything I saw.I don't doubt for a second that Spurlock gained weight during his experiment. But I don't buy for a second that eating at McDonald's made him vomit. Nor do I buy the idea that he'd suffer from impotence or develop a life-threatening liver condition after just a few weeks. In short, I think Spurlock and his doctors are liars.That's not to say their overall points aren't valid. Again, eating junk food on a regular basis DOES have bad long-term consequences. But a month is not the long term! It certainly isn't long enough for any serious health effects to show themselves. And that, I think, is why Spurlock felt the need to exaggerate and distort the truth.Mind you, I think Spurlock is a decent person, and I'm sure he is lying with the best and most noble of intentions, just as many anti-smoking crusaders lie about the effects of smoking second-hand smoke. They're so sure that they're right, and they're so sure of the righteousness of their cause, they can't see how ANY tactic could be wrong.If Spurlock were here with me, I suspect he'd tell me, confidentailly, "Okay, okay, so I exaggerated a bit. So I staged the vomit scene. So I got the doctor to overstate the damage to my liver. I admit, I'm a little bit of a fraud. But where's the harm? If I manage to scare some people into eating less junk, that's a GOOD thing, isn't it?"Well, no. Lying isn't a good thing, even if the desired end result is a noble one. That's why, even though Spurlock is a likeable, charming and even funny guy, I can't recommend this movie. At all.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The assumption behind this documentary is that consumers do not know what is good for them. McDonalds has succeeded in brainwashing millions through its advertising. 		<date>2010-07-04</date>
		<username>sddavis63 (revsdd@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>A Somewhat Pointless Documentary</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>No one - least of all me - is going to disagree with what Morgan Spurlock discovered as a result of this documentary. I will, however, wonder why he bothered. Let's see. He discovered that if you eat nothing but McDonald's food for a month - breakfast, lunch and dinner for 30 days - you'll get sick. That's hardly earth-shaking news. He discovered that there are a lot of obese Americans. Walk through any shopping mall in the United States and you can see that. He discovered that the fast food industry tries to get us hooked on their food. For those earth-shaking discoveries I needed a documentary? Basically, that's why I didn't care for this. It failed to do the thing that a documentary should do: it didn't teach me anything. That left me wondering what Spurlock was really hoping to accomplish. He seemed sincere. He really seemed to want to know what would happen if he proceeded with this insane experiment. In fairness, the doctors he consulted seemed to suggest that they didn't think the all McDonald's diet would have such an impact on his health - which (I'm sorry) makes me question their medical credentials! But to me this was like running an experiment to see if water will evaporate when the temperature reaches the boiling point of water! But - as obvious as this was - Spurlock persists in the experimental diet even after all of his doctors tell him to stop. If I'm questioning the doctors' credentials at this point, I'm questioning Spurlock's sanity as well.He seemed to be trying to put together a Michael Moore-type of quasi-documentary, but in all honesty he's nowhere near as good. Moore - while he makes lousy documentaries (because what's he really makes is propaganda) - at least manages to put out stuff that's both interesting and entertaining. I found this dull because the message was so obvious, and the experiment so absolutely over the top ridiculous. Will very many people give up fast food because of this? Not likely. The fast food addicts will say "well - duh - you're not supposed to eat it three times a day for a month," and they'll continue merrily eating it 5 times a week. As the movie closes, Spurlock seems to be taking credit for a decision by McDonald's to stop "super-sizing" because it happened a few weeks after the movie. I'll give him the benefit of the doubt on that. We'll say the movie forced McDonald's hand. This is still a pretty silly documentary!</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Wait - fast food is BAD?!? Surely not!!! And there's MORE!?!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just let me say I'm not a fan of Micheal Moore and his relentless pursuit of sitting ducks in one-note diatribes mounted with the sophistication of an emotionally stunted 12-year-old supercharged on additives. Fortunately for the "Super Size Me" crew, though, it means their only major competition is a fame-hungry hysteric who looks like he INVENTED "Super Size", so Morgan Spurlock walks away looking like a philosophy professor.This isn't award-winning or life-changing cinema, it's an entertaining and mildly informative anti-corporate stunt. It avoids the worst aspects of Moore's catch-all strategy of taking a film crew to someone's office or home and shouting them until the audience starts to sympathies with the Devil. Spurlock's notional target is McDonalds, but when he fails to arrange an interview with any senior executives he preserves his dignity by choosing not to pursue them to their beds with a loudhailer.The film is notionally a reaction to a court case in which two obese American teenagers tried to sue McDonalds for making them fat. The film attacks McDonalds's claims about the healthiness of its food with a subtlety only usually seen in Adam Sandler movies. The obvious pitch and tag-line is Spurlock's 30-day McDonalds diet, a childish stunt that is actually an effective smoke-screen for most of the more serious underlying issues he raises.There are a couple of real triumphs in the film. The lawyer for the two obese teens is literally unable to think of a better motive for the case than the potential cash compensation. An American food industry marketing spokesman slips up and admits "we are part of the problem", and by the time the film was released was out of a job. We're told that most American schools use "lowest bidder" catering giants to provide horrifically unhealthy meals for children, or serve slop that arrives in cartons for the school kitchen staff to nuke and serve. We see how school meals cooked on site using unprocessed ingredients can actually improve the behaviour of "troubled" children. We also discover that most of the American population who are not rocket (or food) scientists have no idea what a calorie is.There are some nightmarishly wrong-headed moments, like the extended sequence showing how a potentially terminal diabetic the size of a small house, who used to drink 2 gallons of "soda" a day, has most of his stomach surgically removed. An inarticulate bag of lard is embarrassed on a global scale by her pointless recriminations against the sinister character peddling something called the "Subway Diet", an apparent promise to make you thin if you eat at one of McDonalds fast food franchise rivals. And there is always the McDonalds diet itself, a New York slacker's idea of subversive intellectual terrorism which doesn't so much form a thread from which to hang the other stories as dominate everything pretty much to their exclusion.I mean, what is the water cooler moment for this movie? The school meal technician whose favourite cooking tool is the blade she uses to slice open boxes of pre-cooked stodge? Or the REALLY COOL part where the guy just, like, hurls his Super Size double quarter pounder into the parking lot?On the whole it was fun, and showed the give and take of global scale corporations. McDonalds won their day in court, a judge decided they officially didn't make the girls fat, so they dropped "Super Size" from their menu. The film-makers get a pat on the back from their liberal peers, a great distribution deal, and a watered-down anti-globalisation message into the marketplace. Occasional fast food customers lose the chance of getting a better-than-snack-size portion for a couple of pennies, and no-one even pauses to wonder would McDonalds have really dropped "Super Size" if it wasn't just a loss-leader with a microscopic impact on their bottom line?</fullReview>
	</review>
